Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
8 min
Prohibition on Selling Orphan's Real Estate and Its Exceptions
Rulings on exceptions to prohibition of selling orphan's real estate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the seven exceptions when an executor may sell an orphan's real estate and apply the rule to a new scenario.

Core Idea
Executor cannot sell orphan's real estate unless one of seven strict exceptions applies.

This passage from the Book of Wills establishes a fundamental restriction: the executor (wasi) must not sell the orphan’s real estate. The early Hanafi scholars (mutawwalūn) and later scholars (mutā'akhkhirūn) agreed on this prohibition, but they carved out seven specific exceptions. Understanding these exceptions is critical for correct fatwa issuance, as the mufti must identify whether a given case falls within an exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The executor cannot sell the orphan's real estate. This is the default prohibition.
This rule applies regardless of the executor's good intentions.
2
List the first three exceptions from al-Zayla'i
Al-Zayla'i mentions three exceptions: selling at double the value, orphan needs maintenance, and debt on the deceased.
These are found in the text starting with 'كما ذكره الزيلعي'.
3
Add the next three from al-Zahirah
The author adds three more from al-Zahirah: suspended will, proceeds insufficient, and risk of deterioration.
These are listed after 'ثلاث من الظهيرية'.
4
Add the fourth from Buyu' al-Khaniyyah
The last exception is when the property is in the hands of a usurper and the executor fears loss.
This is from بيوع الخانية.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Given a new situation, check if it matches any of the seven exceptions. If not, the sale is prohibited.
Practice by testing cases like 'orphan has no food but owns a house'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوصايا لا يجوز للوصي بيع عقار اليتيم عند المتقدمين ومنعه المتأخرون أيضًا إلا في ثلاثة كما ذكره الزيلعي : إذا بيع بضعف قيمته، وفيما إذا احتاج اليتيم إلى النفقة ولا مال له سواه، وفيما إذا كان على الميت دين لا وفاء له إلا منه . وزدت أربعًا فصار المستثنى سبعًا ؛ ثلاث من الظهيرية فيما إذا كان في التَّرِكَة وصية مرسلة لا نفاذ لها إلا منه، وفيما إذا كانت غلاته لا تزيد على مؤنته، وفيما إذا كان حانوتا أو دارًا يخشى عليه النقصان . (انتهى). فله والرابعة من بيوع الخانية : فيما إذا كان العقار في يد متغلب وخاف الوصي عليه بیعه (انتهى). وفي المجمع ويضمّ القاضي إلى العاجز مَن يعينه فإن شكى إليه ذلك لا يجيبه حتى يتحققه فإن ظهر عجزه استبدل به وإن شكى منه الورثة لا يعزله حتى تظهر له خيانة . (انتهى). وفيه وبيع الوصي من اليتيم أو شراؤه لنفسه وفيه نفع للصبي جائز . (انتهى). واختلفوا في تفسير النفع فقيل نقصان النصف في البيع وفي الشراء بزيادة نصف القيمة وقيل در همان في العشرة نقصانا وزيادة وتمامه في وصايا الخانية، وقسمة مالاً مشتركا بينه وبين الصغير، تجوز إن كان فيها نفع ظاهر عند الإمام خلافا الوصي، لمحمد رحمه الله تعالى؛ كذا في قسمة القنية. وفي جامع الفصولين : قضى وصيَّه دينا بغير أمر القاضي فلما كبر اليتيم أنكر دينًا على أبيه ضمن وصيه ما دفعه لو لم يجد ينة . إذا أقر بسبب الضمان وهو الدفع إلى الأجنبي فلو ظهر غريم آخر يغرم له حصته لدفعه باختياره بعض حقه إلى غيره فلو لم تكن للغريم الأول بينة على الدين يضمن الوصي كل ما دفعه إليه لوقوعه بغير حجة. وصي أدَّى دَينا فأنكرت الورثة تقبل بينته ولولا بينة فله تحليف الورثة. (انتهى). فقد علم أن الوصي لا يقبل قوله في قضاء دين على الميت سواء كان المنازع له اليتيم بعد بلوغ أو لا ، إلا في مهر المرأة فإنه لا ضمان عليه إذا دفعه بلا بينة كما في خزانة المفتين. وقيَّده في جامع الفصولين على قول بالمؤجل عرفًا. وفي بيع القنية : ولو باع القاضي من وصي الميت شيئًا من التَّرِكَة بثمن لا ينفذ لأنه محجور به. والوصي لا يملك الشراء لنفسه ولو اشتراه القاضي لنفسه من الوصي الذي نصبه عن الميت جاز (انتهى).
Book of Wills. It is not permissible for the executor to sell the orphan's real estate according to the early scholars, and the later scholars also prohibited it except in three cases as mentioned by al-Zayla'i: when it is sold for double its value, when the orphan needs maintenance and has no other property, and when there is a debt on the deceased that cannot be paid except from it. I added four more, so the exceptions become seven: three from al-Zahira: when the estate contains a suspended will that cannot be executed except from it, when its proceeds do not exceed its expenses, and when it is a shop or house that is feared to deteriorate. (End). And the fourth from Buyu' al-Khaniyyah: when the real estate is in the hand of a usurper and the executor fears for it. (End). And in al-Majma': The judge appoints an assistant to the incompetent executor; if the executor complains, the judge does not respond until he verifies. If his incompetence appears, the judge replaces him; if the heirs complain, the judge does not dismiss him until evidence of treachery appears. (End). And in it: The executor's sale to the orphan or purchase from himself in which there is benefit for the child is permissible. (End). They differed on the interpretation of benefit: it is said a reduction of half in sale and an increase of half in purchase; and it is said two dirhams per ten, increase and reduction. The full discussion is in Wasaya al-Khaniyyah. The division of common property between him and the child is permitted if it contains apparent benefit according to Imam Abu Hanifa, contrary to the executor, and to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him); thus in Qismat al-Qunyah. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: The executor paid a debt without the judge's order; when the orphan matured and denied a debt on his father, the executor is liable for what he paid if he has no evidence. If he admits liability due to payment to a stranger, then if another creditor appears, the executor pays him his share because he paid one creditor voluntarily. If the first creditor has no evidence of the debt, the executor is liable for all he paid because it was without justification. An executor paid a debt and the heirs denied; his evidence is accepted, and if no evidence, he may have the heirs take an oath. (End). So it is known that the executor's statement is not accepted regarding payment of a debt on the deceased, whether the contestant is the orphan after maturity or not, except for the woman's dower, where he is not liable if he paid it without evidence, as in Khizanat al-Muftin. And it is restricted in Jami' al-Fusulayn to the deferred dower by custom. In Bay' al-Qunyah: If the judge sells something from the estate to the deceased's executor for a price, it is not effective because the executor is restricted. The executor has no right to buy for himself; but if the judge buys it for himself from the executor appointed by the deceased, it is permissible. (End).
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a set of rules regarding the executor's (wasi) authority over the orphan's property, particularly real estate. It begins by stating the established prohibition: early and later scholars agree that the executor cannot sell the orphan's real estate except in three exceptional cases listed by al-Zayla'i: (1) selling at double the value to benefit the orphan, (2) when the orphan needs maintenance and has no other funds, and (3) when there is a debt on the deceased that can only be paid from that property. The author adds four more exceptions from other sources: (a) a suspended will that cannot be executed otherwise, (b) when the property's income does not cover its maintenance, (c) when the property (shop/house) is at risk of deterioration, and (d) when the property is in the hands of a usurper and the executor fears loss. The text then addresses procedural matters: the judge may appoint an assistant to an incompetent executor, but only after verification; the executor may purchase from or sell to the orphan if there is clear benefit. A dispute is noted on the definition of 'benefit': some say half the value, others say two dirhams per ten. The division of common property is allowed if beneficial according to Abu Hanifa, but Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees. Further, the executor's payment of debts without court order is risky: if the orphan later denies the debt, the executor is liable unless he has evidence. The executor's statement about paying debts is generally not accepted, except for a woman's dower paid without evidence (customary deferred dower). Finally, the judge cannot sell estate property to the executor, but the judge can buy it himself from the executor.

Key Terms
الوصي (al-wasi): The executor appointed by the deceased in a will, who manages the estate and cares for orphan children.
اليتيم (al-yateem): An orphan minor child who has lost his father before reaching puberty.
النفع (al-naf'): Benefit to the orphan, specifically a financial advantage in transactions (e.g., buying below market or selling above).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the exceptions for selling orphan's real estate?
A) Sale at double value.
B) Orphan needs maintenance.
C) Orphan's parent agrees.
D) Risk of deterioration.

Key Takeaway
Executor may sell orphan's real estate only in seven strictly defined exceptions.

Definition of Benefit (Naf') and Dispute on Division of Common Property
Definitions of benefit and dispute on dividing common property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two definitions of benefit (naf') and state Abu Hanifa's position on dividing common property between executor and orphan.

Core Idea
Benefit to orphan is defined either as half-value increase or two dirhams per ten; division is allowed if benefit apparent.

The passage next discusses transactions between executor and orphan where benefit (naf') is required. There is a difference of opinion on how much benefit constitutes sufficient advantage. Additionally, the division of common property between executor and orphan is allowed according to Abu Hanifa if apparent benefit exists, but Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees. Understanding this intra-madhhab dispute is essential for tarjih in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the rule
The executor can buy from or sell to the orphan if there is benefit (naf') to the orphan.
This is stated: 'وفيه وبيع الوصي من اليتيم أو شراؤه لنفسه وفيه نفع للصبي جائز'.
2
Identify the two definitions of naf'
The passage records two definitions: half the value (increase or reduction) and two dirhams per ten (i.e., 20% change).
The disagreement is noted as 'واختلفوا في تفسير النفع'.
3
Note the dispute on division of common property
Abu Hanifa permits division of common property between executor and orphan if there is apparent benefit. Muhammad al-Shaybani holds it is not permissible.
The text says 'خلافا الوصي، لمحمد' meaning contrary to the executor and to Muhammad.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if the judge deems it beneficial, division is valid according to Abu Hanifa but not according to Muhammad.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's position.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa, allowing division with apparent benefit.
This is indicated by the tarjih note in the analytical toolbox.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to the passage, what is the position of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani on dividing common property between executor and orphan?
A) Permissible if benefit apparent.
B) Prohibited absolutely.
C) Permissible only if authorized by judge.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

10 min
Procedural Rules and Liability for Executor's Debt Payments
Rules on executor's liability for debt payment without court order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an executor is liable for paying a deceased's debt without court order, and state the exception for woman's dower.

Core Idea
Executor who pays a debt without court order bears liability if the orphan later denies the debt.

This section deals with practical procedures: the executor's payment of debts, the burden of proof, and the judge's role in overseeing executors. The passage establishes that an executor is generally liable if he pays a debt without evidence or court order, except for the woman's dower which is customarily deferred.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general rule
If an executor pays a debt without court order, he is liable if the orphan later denies the debt and he has no evidence.
The text: 'قضى وصيَّه دينا بغير أمر القاضي فلما كبر اليتيم أنكر دينًا على أبيه ضمن وصيه ما دفعه لو لم يجد ينة'.
2
Note the exception for woman's dower
The executor is not liable for paying the woman's dower without evidence, if it is the deferred dower by custom.
From Khizanat al-Muftin: 'إلا في مهر المرأة فإنه لا ضمان عليه إذا دفعه بلا بينة'.
3
Identify when the executor is liable in case of multiple creditors
If an executor pays one creditor without evidence and another creditor appears, the executor must pay the second as well.
The text: 'فلو ظهر غريم آخر يغرم له حصته'.
4
Understand the judge's oversight
The judge may appoint an assistant to an incompetent executor, and may dismiss him only after verifying treachery.
This is from the beginning of the section: 'ويضمّ القاضي إلى العاجز مَن يعينه'.
5
Apply to a scenario
Given a new case, decide whether the executor is liable based on whether he had evidence or court order.
Practice by testing cases like 'executor pays a debt on oral instruction'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوصايا لا يجوز للوصي بيع عقار اليتيم عند المتقدمين ومنعه المتأخرون أيضًا إلا في ثلاثة كما ذكره الزيلعي : إذا بيع بضعف قيمته، وفيما إذا احتاج اليتيم إلى النفقة ولا مال له سواه، وفيما إذا كان على الميت دين لا وفاء له إلا منه . وزدت أربعًا فصار المستثنى سبعًا ؛ ثلاث من الظهيرية فيما إذا كان في التَّرِكَة وصية مرسلة لا نفاذ لها إلا منه، وفيما إذا كانت غلاته لا تزيد على مؤنته، وفيما إذا كان حانوتا أو دارًا يخشى عليه النقصان . (انتهى). فله والرابعة من بيوع الخانية : فيما إذا كان العقار في يد متغلب وخاف الوصي عليه بیعه (انتهى). وفي المجمع ويضمّ القاضي إلى العاجز مَن يعينه فإن شكى إليه ذلك لا يجيبه حتى يتحققه فإن ظهر عجزه استبدل به وإن شكى منه الورثة لا يعزله حتى تظهر له خيانة . (انتهى). وفيه وبيع الوصي من اليتيم أو شراؤه لنفسه وفيه نفع للصبي جائز . (انتهى). واختلفوا في تفسير النفع فقيل نقصان النصف في البيع وفي الشراء بزيادة نصف القيمة وقيل در همان في العشرة نقصانا وزيادة وتمامه في وصايا الخانية، وقسمة مالاً مشتركا بينه وبين الصغير، تجوز إن كان فيها نفع ظاهر عند الإمام خلافا الوصي، لمحمد رحمه الله تعالى؛ كذا في قسمة القنية. وفي جامع الفصولين : قضى وصيَّه دينا بغير أمر القاضي فلما كبر اليتيم أنكر دينًا على أبيه ضمن وصيه ما دفعه لو لم يجد ينة . إذا أقر بسبب الضمان وهو الدفع إلى الأجنبي فلو ظهر غريم آخر يغرم له حصته لدفعه باختياره بعض حقه إلى غيره فلو لم تكن للغريم الأول بينة على الدين يضمن الوصي كل ما دفعه إليه لوقوعه بغير حجة. وصي أدَّى دَينا فأنكرت الورثة تقبل بينته ولولا بينة فله تحليف الورثة. (انتهى). فقد علم أن الوصي لا يقبل قوله في قضاء دين على الميت سواء كان المنازع له اليتيم بعد بلوغ أو لا ، إلا في مهر المرأة فإنه لا ضمان عليه إذا دفعه بلا بينة كما في خزانة المفتين. وقيَّده في جامع الفصولين على قول بالمؤجل عرفًا. وفي بيع القنية : ولو باع القاضي من وصي الميت شيئًا من التَّرِكَة بثمن لا ينفذ لأنه محجور به. والوصي لا يملك الشراء لنفسه ولو اشتراه القاضي لنفسه من الوصي الذي نصبه عن الميت جاز (انتهى).
Book of Wills. It is not permissible for the executor to sell the orphan's real estate according to the early scholars, and the later scholars also prohibited it except in three cases as mentioned by al-Zayla'i: when it is sold for double its value, when the orphan needs maintenance and has no other property, and when there is a debt on the deceased that cannot be paid except from it. I added four more, so the exceptions become seven: three from al-Zahira: when the estate contains a suspended will that cannot be executed except from it, when its proceeds do not exceed its expenses, and when it is a shop or house that is feared to deteriorate. (End). And the fourth from Buyu' al-Khaniyyah: when the real estate is in the hand of a usurper and the executor fears for it. (End). And in al-Majma': The judge appoints an assistant to the incompetent executor; if the executor complains, the judge does not respond until he verifies. If his incompetence appears, the judge replaces him; if the heirs complain, the judge does not dismiss him until evidence of treachery appears. (End). And in it: The executor's sale to the orphan or purchase from himself in which there is benefit for the child is permissible. (End). They differed on the interpretation of benefit: it is said a reduction of half in sale and an increase of half in purchase; and it is said two dirhams per ten, increase and reduction. The full discussion is in Wasaya al-Khaniyyah. The division of common property between him and the child is permitted if it contains apparent benefit according to Imam Abu Hanifa, contrary to the executor, and to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him); thus in Qismat al-Qunyah. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: The executor paid a debt without the judge's order; when the orphan matured and denied a debt on his father, the executor is liable for what he paid if he has no evidence. If he admits liability due to payment to a stranger, then if another creditor appears, the executor pays him his share because he paid one creditor voluntarily. If the first creditor has no evidence of the debt, the executor is liable for all he paid because it was without justification. An executor paid a debt and the heirs denied; his evidence is accepted, and if no evidence, he may have the heirs take an oath. (End). So it is known that the executor's statement is not accepted regarding payment of a debt on the deceased, whether the contestant is the orphan after maturity or not, except for the woman's dower, where he is not liable if he paid it without evidence, as in Khizanat al-Muftin. And it is restricted in Jami' al-Fusulayn to the deferred dower by custom. In Bay' al-Qunyah: If the judge sells something from the estate to the deceased's executor for a price, it is not effective because the executor is restricted. The executor has no right to buy for himself; but if the judge buys it for himself from the executor appointed by the deceased, it is permissible. (End).
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a set of rules regarding the executor's (wasi) authority over the orphan's property, particularly real estate. It begins by stating the established prohibition: early and later scholars agree that the executor cannot sell the orphan's real estate except in three exceptional cases listed by al-Zayla'i: (1) selling at double the value to benefit the orphan, (2) when the orphan needs maintenance and has no other funds, and (3) when there is a debt on the deceased that can only be paid from that property. The author adds four more exceptions from other sources: (a) a suspended will that cannot be executed otherwise, (b) when the property's income does not cover its maintenance, (c) when the property (shop/house) is at risk of deterioration, and (d) when the property is in the hands of a usurper and the executor fears loss. The text then addresses procedural matters: the judge may appoint an assistant to an incompetent executor, but only after verification; the executor may purchase from or sell to the orphan if there is clear benefit. A dispute is noted on the definition of 'benefit': some say half the value, others say two dirhams per ten. The division of common property is allowed if beneficial according to Abu Hanifa, but Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees. Further, the executor's payment of debts without court order is risky: if the orphan later denies the debt, the executor is liable unless he has evidence. The executor's statement about paying debts is generally not accepted, except for a woman's dower paid without evidence (customary deferred dower). Finally, the judge cannot sell estate property to the executor, but the judge can buy it himself from the executor.

Key Terms
الوصي (al-wasi): The executor appointed by the deceased in a will, who manages the estate and cares for orphan children.
اليتيم (al-yateem): An orphan minor child who has lost his father before reaching puberty.
النفع (al-naf'): Benefit to the orphan, specifically a financial advantage in transactions (e.g., buying below market or selling above).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which case is the executor NOT liable for paying a debt without evidence?
A) Ordinary trade debt.
B) Medical expenses.
C) Woman's dower.
D) Rent.

Key Takeaway
Executor should obtain court order or evidence for debt payments, except for the woman's customarily deferred dower.

Seven Exceptions to Executor Selling Orphan's Real Estate
Enumeration of exceptions to the prohibition of selling orphan's property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the seven exceptions when the Hanafi executor may sell the orphan's real estate and apply the conditions for each exception.

Core Idea
An executor cannot sell an orphan's real estate unless one of seven specific exceptions applies.

You already know that the Hanafi school generally prohibits the executor from selling an orphan's immovable property. However, fiqh is not rigid; there are necessary exceptions. This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir enumerates seven such exceptions compiled from multiple authoritative sources. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for a mufti faced with an orphan's need.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
The default ruling: the executor (wasi) is not permitted to sell the orphan's real estate (aqar).
This is the established position of both earlier and later Hanafi scholars.
2
Recall the first three exceptions from al-Zayla'i
Identify the three cases: sale at double value, orphan's maintenance need with no other wealth, and deceased's debt that can only be paid from the property.
These exceptions involve clear financial benefit or necessity.
3
Recall the next three exceptions from al-Dhahiriyya
Identify: unexecuted will that can only be fulfilled from the property, property income insufficient for expenses, and a shop or house at risk of deterioration.
These address ongoing obligations or property preservation.
4
Recall the seventh exception from al-Khaniyya
When the property is in the hands of a usurper (mutaghallib) and the executor fears for its safety.
This is a precautionary measure to prevent loss.
5
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Given a specific case, determine whether an exception applies and which one.
Practice matching facts to conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يجوز للوصي بيع عقار اليتيم عند المتقدمين ومنعه المتأخرون أيضًا إلا في ثلاثة كما ذكره الزيلعي : إذا بيع بضعف قيمته، وفيما إذا احتاج اليتيم إلى النفقة ولا مال له سواه، وفيما إذا كان على الميت دين لا وفاء له إلا منه . وزدت أربعًا فصار المستثنى سبعًا ؛ ثلاث من الظهيرية فيما إذا كان في التَّرِكَة وصية مرسلة لا نفاذ لها إلا منه، وفيما إذا كانت غلاته لا تزيد على مؤنته، وفيما إذا كان حانوتا أو دارًا يخشى عليه النقصان . (انتهى). فله والرابعة من بيوع الخانية : فيما إذا كان العقار في يد متغلب وخاف الوصي عليه بیعه (انتهى).
It is not permissible for the executor to sell the real estate of the orphan according to the earlier scholars, and the later ones also forbid it except in three cases as mentioned by al-Zayla'i: when it is sold for double its value, when the orphan needs maintenance and has no other property, and when the deceased has a debt that can only be paid from it. I added four more, so the exceptions become seven: three from al-Dhahiriyya: when there is an unexecuted will in the estate that can only be fulfilled from it, when its revenues do not exceed its expenses, and when it is a shop or house whose deterioration is feared. (End). And the fourth from the sales of al-Khaniyya: when the property is in the hand of an oppressor and the executor fears for it, he may sell it. (End).
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on the executor's authority to sell the orphan's immovable property. The earlier scholars (mutaqaddimun) and later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) generally forbid such a sale, except in seven specific cases. The first three are from al-Zayla'i: 1) if the property can be sold at double its value (a clear benefit); 2) if the orphan needs maintenance and has no other wealth; 3) if the deceased had debts that cannot be paid otherwise. The author added four more: three from the Dhahiriyya works: when there is a bequest that can only be fulfilled from that property, when the property's income is less than its running costs, and when it is a shop or house at risk of deterioration. The fourth added exception is from al-Khaniyya: if the property is in the hands of a usurper and the executor fears losing it. These seven exceptions allow the sale despite the general prohibition.

Key Terms
الوصي (al-wasi): The executor appointed by the testator or by the judge to manage the orphan's affairs.
عقار (aqar): Immovable property such as land, houses, shops.
الظهيرية (al-Dhahiriyya): A Hanafi work or set of rulings; source of the three additional exceptions.
الخانية (al-Khaniyya): A Hanafi compilation of rulings, specifically 'Biyu' al-Khaniyya' (Sales of al-Khaniyya).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which of the following cases is the Hanafi executor allowed to sell the orphan's real estate?
A) Whenever the executor thinks it is profitable.
B) If the orphan needs maintenance and has no other wealth.
C) If the property is worth less than its tax.
D) If the executor wants to invest the proceeds.

Key Takeaway
An executor may only sell orphan's real estate when one of seven specific conditions is verified.

Executor's Transactions with Orphan: Condition of Benefit (Naf')
Explaining the condition of benefit in executor's transactions with orphan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an executor may transact with an orphan only if there is manifest benefit, identify the two definitions of benefit (half or tenth), and distinguish Abu Hanifa's position from Imam Muhammad's on division of common property.

Core Idea
Executor's sale to orphan requires manifest benefit — defined as half or tenth above or below market value.

This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir addresses a critical fiduciary rule: the executor (wasi) of an orphan must not benefit personally from transactions with the orphan's property. The Hanafi school conditions validity on a clear, measurable advantage (naf'), with different interpretations of what constitutes 'manifest benefit.' We also see an intra-madhhab dispute on dividing jointly owned property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transactions
The passage covers two types of dealings: (1) sale to or purchase from the orphan, and (2) division of common property (qisma) between executor and orphan.
2
Interpret the benefit threshold
Benefit (naf') is defined quantitatively: in a sale, a reduction of half the market value; in a purchase, an increase of half the value. An alternative view applies the same percentage to a tenth.
The phrase 'در همان في العشرة' (Persian) indicates 'the same in ten'—the half-rule applied to a tenth.
3
Analyze the division dispute
Imam Abu Hanifa permits dividing common property between executor and orphan if there is manifest benefit (naf' zahir). Imam Muhammad disallows it. The relied-upon view is Abu Hanifa's, as recorded in al-Qunyah.
The dispute likely stems from differing degrees of caution against conflict of interest.
4
Determine the operative ruling
For fatwa, Abu Hanifa's position is mufta bihi: division is valid with manifest benefit. The definition of benefit (half or tenth) is a matter of ijtihad, and both are acceptable if benefit is clear.
The passage refers to Wasiyya al-Khaniyya for full details on benefit definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في تفسير النفع فقيل نقصان النصف في البيع وفي الشراء بزيادة نصف القيمة وقيل در همان في العشرة نقصانا وزيادة وتمامه في وصايا الخانية، وقسمة مالاً مشتركا بينه وبين الصغير، تجوز إن كان فيها نفع ظاهر عند الإمام خلافا • الوصي، لمحمد رحمه الله تعالى؛ كذا في قسمة القنية.
They differed regarding the interpretation of benefit. It was said: a reduction of half in the sale, and an increase of half the value in the purchase. And it was said: the same in ten, reduction and increase. Its completion is in the Wasiyya of al-Khaniyya. And the division of common property between him (the executor) and the minor is permissible if there is manifest benefit in it according to Imam Abu Hanifa, contrary to Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him. Thus in the Division of al-Qunyah.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage discusses the condition of benefit (naf') when the executor sells to or buys from the orphan. There is a disagreement on what constitutes benefit: some say a reduction of half the price in a sale (i.e., buying at half market value) and an increase of half in a purchase. Others say the same principle applies to a tenth. The full details are in Wasiyya al-Khaniyya. The passage then addresses the division of common property between the executor and the minor (orphan). Abu Hanifa allows it if there is manifest (zahir) benefit, while Imam Muhammad does not allow it. The ruling is recorded in Qismat al-Qunyah.

Key Terms
النفع (al-naf'): Benefit or advantage; in this context, a measurable financial benefit in transactions with the orphan.
قسمة (qisma): Division of jointly owned property into separate shares.
الوصي (al-wasi): Executor or guardian appointed to manage the affairs of an orphan or minor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when can an executor divide common property with the orphan?
A) Whenever the executor wants to separate the shares.
B) Only if there is manifest benefit, according to Abu Hanifa.
C) Only if the judge approves.
D) It is never allowed.

Key Takeaway
An executor may sell, purchase, or divide property with an orphan only if there is clear, quantifiable benefit, defined as half or tenth of the value.

Twelve Exceptions to the Executor's Believability
List of exceptions with fatwa examples and a maxim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the twelve exceptions to the rule that an executor's claim is accepted in matters he is authorized over.

Core Idea
The executor is believed only in matters within his authority; twelve specific claims require proof or are subject to dispute.

As a mufti, you will encounter disputes between executors and heirs or orphans over financial claims. This passage from Fatawa al-Atabi provides a crucial rule: generally, the executor's word is accepted in matters he was authorized to handle, but there are twelve specific exceptions where his claim is not accepted without proof. Understanding these exceptions is essential for issuing correct fatwas in inheritance and trust cases.

Guided Path
1
Understand the General Principle
Identify the overarching rule: an executor is believed in what he is authorized over. This is the baseline for evaluating his claims.
The principle is stated at the end of the passage: 'كل شيء كان مسلطا عليه فإنه يصدق فيه وما لا فلا'.
2
Parse the Twelve Exceptions
List each of the twelve cases where the executor's claim is not accepted without proof. Note the specific scenario and why it falls outside his authority.
The exceptions range from paying the deceased's debt to claiming dower payment for the orphan's marriage.
3
Distinguish Agreed vs. Disputed Exceptions
Among the exceptions, some are agreed upon (e.g., maintenance of the orphan's mahram), while two are disputed between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (kharaj payment and slave reward). Note the difference.
The text says three cases by agreement? Actually, one agreed (mahram), two disputed.
4
Apply to a New Scenario
Given a hypothetical query, determine whether the executor's claim falls under the general rule or an exception. Use the maxim and the list.
For example, if the executor claims he bought a new house for the orphan without prior authorization, that likely requires proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي جامع الفصولين : قضى وصيَّه دينا بغير أمر القاضي فلما كبر اليتيم أنكر دينًا على أبيه ضمن وصيه ما دفعه لو لم يجد ينة . إذا أقر بسبب الضمان وهو الدفع إلى الأجنبي فلو ظهر غريم آخر يغرم له حصته لدفعه باختياره بعض حقه إلى غيره فلو لم تكن للغريم الأول بينة على الدين يضمن الوصي كل ما دفعه إليه لوقوعه بغير حجة. وصي أدَّى دَينا فأنكرت الورثة تقبل بينته ولولا بينة فله تحليف الورثة. (انتهى). فقد علم أن الوصي لا يقبل قوله في قضاء دين على الميت سواء كان المنازع له اليتيم بعد بلوغ أو لا ، إلا في مهر المرأة فإنه لا ضمان عليه إذا دفعه بلا بينة كما في خزانة المفتين. وقيَّده في جامع الفصولين على قول بالمؤجل عُرفًا. وفي بيع القنية : ولو باع القاضي من وصي الميت شيئًا من التَّرِكَة بثمن لا ينفذ لأنه محجور به. والوصي لا يملك الشراء لنفسه ولو اشتراه القاضي لنفسه من الوصي الذي نصبه عن الميت جاز (انتهى). وفي الملتقط أنفق الوصي على الموصى في حياته وهو معتقل اللسان يضمن، ولو أنفق الوكيل لا يضمن ولو ادعى الوصي بعد بلوغ اليتيم أنه كان باع عبده وأنفق ثمنه صدق إن كان هالكًا وإلا لا كذا في دعوى خزانة الأكمل. ويقبل قول الوصي فيما يدعيه من الإنفاق بلا بينة إلا في ثلاث؛ في واحدة اتفاقا وهي فيما إذا فرض القاضي نفقة ذي الرحم المحرم على اليتيم فادَّعى الوصي الدفع كذا في شرح المجمع معللاً بأن هذا ليس من حوائج اليتيم وإنما يقبل قوله فيما إذا كان من حوائجه. (انتهى). فينبغي أن تكون نفقة زوجته كذلك لأنها من حوائجه ولا يشكل عليه قبول قول الناظر فيما يدعيه من الصرف على المستحقين بلا بينة لأن هذا جملة عمله من في الوقف وفي ثنتين اختلاف لو قال أديت خراج أرضه أو جعل عبده الآبق . قال أبو يوسف رحمه الله : لا بيان عليه. وقال محمد رحمه الله : عليه البيان، كما في المجمع. والحاصل أن الوصي يقبل قوله فيما يدعيه إلا في مسائل : الأولى : ادعى قضاء دين الميت. الثانية : أدعى أن اليتيم استهلك ماك آخر فدفع ضمانه . الثالثة : ادَّعى أنه أدَّى جعل عبده الآبق من غير إجارة. الرابعة : ادَّعى أنه أذى خراج أرضه في وقت لا تصلح للزراعة. الخامسة : ادعى الإنفاق على محرم اليتيم. السادسة : ادَّعى أنه أذِنَ لليتيم في الإجارة وأنه ركبته ديون فقضاها عنه . السابعة : ادعى الإنفاق عليه من مال نفسه حال غيبة ماله وأراد الرجوع. الثامنة : ادعى الإنفاق على رقيقه الذين ماتوا. التاسعة : اتَّجر وربح ثم ادعى أنه كان مضاربًا. العاشرة : ادعى فداء عبده الجاني. الحادية عشرة : ادعى قضاء دين الميت من ماله بعد بيع التّرِكَة قبل قبض ثمنها . الثانية عشرة : ادعى أنه زوّج اليتيم امرأة ودفع مهرها من ماله وهي ميتة. الكل في فتاوى العتابي من الوصايا وذكر ضابطًا وهو أن كل شيء كان مسلطا عليه فإنه يصدق فيه وما لا فلا.
In Jami' al-Fusulayn: An executor paid a debt without the judge's order. When the orphan came of age and denied a debt of his father, the executor was held liable for what he paid if he did not have proof. If he acknowledged the reason for liability—namely payment to a third party—then if another creditor appeared, he would be made to pay his share because he paid some of his right to another voluntarily. If the first creditor had no proof of the debt, the executor is liable for all he paid because it was without proof. An executor paid a debt and the heirs denied it; his proof is accepted, and without proof he can have the heirs swear. (End). So it is known that the executor's word is not accepted regarding paying a debt of the deceased, whether the opponent is the orphan after majority or not, except for the dower of a woman: he is not liable if he pays without proof, as in Khizanat al-Muftin. But it is restricted in Jami' al-Fusulayn to what is customarily deferred. And in Bay' al-Qunyah: If the judge sells something from the estate to the executor of the deceased at a price, it is not valid because he is interdicted. The executor does not have the right to buy for himself. If the judge buys for himself from the executor appointed by the deceased over the estate, it is permissible. (End). In al-Multaqat: If the executor spent on the testator during his life while he was mute (unable to speak), he is liable. If an agent spent, he is not liable. If the executor claimed after the orphan reached majority that he had sold his slave and spent the price, he is believed if the slave is destroyed, otherwise not—as in Da'wa Khizanat al-Akmal. The executor's word is accepted regarding his claims of expenditure without proof except in three cases: one by agreement—when the judge imposed maintenance for a close relative (mahram) on the orphan and the executor claims to have paid; this is not accepted, as in Sharh al-Majma', because it is not among the orphan's needs; his word is only accepted in matters that are his needs. (End). So the maintenance of his wife should be similar because it is among his needs. And there is no difficulty in accepting the word of the overseer (nazir) regarding claims of spending on entitled recipients without proof, because that is the sum of his work in the waqf. And in two cases there is disagreement: if he said he paid the kharaj of his land or the reward for his runaway slave. Abu Yusuf said: no proof is required. Muhammad said: proof is required. As in al-Majma'. The summary is that the executor's word is accepted in what he claims except in the following matters: 1. Claiming payment of a debt of the deceased. 2. Claiming that the orphan consumed something belonging to another and he paid compensation. 3. Claiming that he paid the reward for the orphan's runaway slave without hire. 4. Claiming that he paid the kharaj of his land at a time unfit for cultivation. 5. Claiming expenditure on the orphan's mahram. 6. Claiming that he permitted the orphan to lease and that debts mounted on him and he paid them. 7. Claiming expenditure on the orphan from his own money during the absence of the orphan's money and seeking reimbursement. 8. Claiming expenditure on his slaves who died. 9. He traded and profited then claimed he was a mudarib (silent partner). 10. Claiming ransom for his offending slave. 11. Claiming payment of a debt of the deceased from his own money after selling the estate but before receiving its price. 12. Claiming that he married the orphan to a woman and paid her dower from his money while she is deceased. All are from Fatawa al-Atabi on Wills, and he mentioned a principle: anything he is authorized over, he is believed; otherwise not.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This extended passage deals with the credibility of the executor's claims regarding financial dealings on behalf of the orphan/deceased. The general rule: the executor's word is accepted in matters he was authorized to handle. However, there are twelve specific exceptions where his claim is not accepted without proof. These include claiming payment of the deceased's debts, claiming compensation for the orphan's consumption of others' property, paying reward for a runaway slave without a hire contract, paying kharaj at a wrong time, spending on mahram relatives, authorizing the orphan's lease and then paying debts, claiming reimbursement for spending his own money, spending on slaves who died, claiming to be a mudarib after earning profit, paying ransom for a slave's offense, paying a debt before receiving sale proceeds, and marrying the orphan and paying dower. The passage also notes disagreements between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on whether proof is needed for kharaj payment and slave reward. The final principle: the executor is believed only in matters within his authority.

Key Terms
الوصي (al-wasi): Executor appointed to manage the orphan's property.
الدين (al-dayn): Debt owed by the deceased or the orphan.
الخراج (al-kharaj): Land tax levied on agricultural land.
الجعل (al-ju'l): Reward (for returning a runaway slave).
يحلف الورثة (yahlif al-wurrath): The executor can have the heirs swear an oath (if he has no proof).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which of the following claims by an executor is NOT accepted without proof?
A) Claiming he spent on the orphan's food and clothing.
B) Claiming he paid the dower for the orphan's wife (the wife is deceased).
C) Claiming he paid the kharaj during the cultivation season.
D) Claiming he paid the reward for a runaway slave with a hire contract.

Key Takeaway
The executor's word is accepted in matters within his authority, but twelve specific exceptions require proof; the mufti must know these to judge disputes fairly.

Eight Distinctions Between Executor of Deceased and Executor of Judge
Eight differences between two types of executors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the eight differences between the executor of the deceased and the executor of the judge, and apply the correct ruling for each scenario.

Core Idea
The testator's executor has broader authority; the judge's executor is tightly controlled.

When a person dies, their estate needs management. The deceased may appoint an executor in their will, or if none exists, a judge may appoint one. While both act as administrators, Hanafi law distinguishes their powers in eight specific ways. Understanding these distinctions is essential for issuing correct fatwas about estate administration and the validity of an executor's actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of executors
Understand that wasi al-mayyit is appointed by the testator, while wasi al-qadi is appointed by the judge. The default rule is that the judge's executor is like the testator's executor, except in eight matters.
This default similarity simplifies the framework.
2
Parse each difference
For each of the eight points, note the specific scenario, the ruling for wasi al-mayyit, and the ruling for wasi al-qadi. Pay attention to the exceptions and any disagreement (e.g., Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn in the first point).
Use the key terms and sources mentioned (Sharh al-Majma', al-Khulasa, etc.).
3
Identify the underlying rationale
Recognize that the testator's executor derives authority from the owner's will and is seen as an extension of the owner, whereas the judge's executor is an agent of the court and thus subject to stricter limits.
This explains why the judge can restrict his own appointee but not the testator's.
4
Note the textual authorities and any contradictions
Each difference is attributed to a specific Hanafi text. Note that for some points there are variant narrations (e.g., in Talkhis al-Jami' the first difference is said to be equal in one narration). Understand how later scholars reconciled such differences.
For example, the fifth point has a disagreement in al-Yatima; reconciliation is achieved via al-Khizana.
5
Apply the rules to a hypothetical case
Given a scenario, determine which executor is involved and which of the eight differences applies. Then state the correct ruling.
Practice strengthens recall and accurate application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاضي كوصي الميت إلا في مسائل : الأولى : أوصى الميت أن يبيع من نفسه ويشتري لنفسه إذا كان فيه نفع ظاهر عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى خلافًا لهما، وأما وصي القاضي فليس له ذلك اتفاقا لأنه كالوكيل وهو لا يعقد لنفسه كذا في شرح المجمع من الوصايا. الثانية : إذا خصَّه القاضي تخصص بخلاف وصي الميت . الثالثة : إذا باع ممَّن لا تقبل شهادته له لم يصح، بخلاف وصي الميت، وهما في الخلاصة وذكر في تلخيص الجامع استواءهما في رواية في الأولى. الرابعة : أوصى الميت أن يؤاجر الصغير بخياطة الذهب وسائر الأعمال، بخلاف وصي القاضي كذا في القنية . الخامسة : ليس للقاضي أن يعزل وصي الميت العدل الكافي، وله عزل وصي القاضي كما في القنية. خلافا لما في اليتيمة . السادسة لا يملك وصي القاضي القبض إلا بإذن مبتدأ من القاضي بعد الإيصاء بخلاف وصي الميت كذا في الخلاصة من المحاضر والسجلات. السابعة : يعمل نهي القاضي عن بعض التصرفات ولا يعمل نهي الميت كما في البزازية وهي راجعة إلى قبول التخصيص وعدمه. الثامنة : وصي القاضي إذا جعل وصيا عند موته لا يصير الثاني وصيا بخلاف وصي الميت كذا في اليتيمة. وفي الخزانة وصي وصي القاضي كوصيه إذا كانت الوصية عامة . .(انتهى) . وبه يحصل التوفيق.
The executor appointed by the judge is like the executor appointed by the deceased except in the following matters: First: If the deceased directed that he sell to himself and buy from himself if there is apparent benefit, according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him), contrary to the two companions. But the executor of the judge does not have that by agreement because he is like an agent, and an agent does not contract with himself. As in Sharh al-Majma' from the chapter on bequests. Second: If the judge restricts him, he becomes restricted, unlike the executor of the deceased. Third: If he sells to someone whose testimony is not acceptable for him, it is invalid, unlike the executor of the deceased. Both are in al-Khulasa, and it is mentioned in Talkhis al-Jami' that they are equal in one narration regarding the first. Fourth: If the deceased directed that he hire out the minor for gold embroidery and all other works, unlike the executor of the judge. As in al-Qunya. Fifth: The judge cannot dismiss the trustworthy and capable executor of the deceased, but he can dismiss the executor of the judge, as in al-Qunya, contrary to what is in al-Yatima. Sixth: The executor of the judge cannot take possession except with new permission from the judge after the appointment, unlike the executor of the deceased, as in al-Khulasa from Muhadir wa Sijillat. Seventh: The prohibition of the judge regarding some transactions is effective, while the prohibition of the deceased is not, as in al-Bazzaziyya, and this goes back to accepting or not accepting restriction. Eighth: If the executor of the judge appoints someone as executor at his death, the second does not become an executor, unlike the executor of the deceased, as in al-Yatima. And in al-Khizana, the executor of the judge's executor is like his executor if the bequest was general. (End). With this reconciliation is achieved.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage delineates eight key differences between two types of executors in Hanafi law: the wasi appointed by the testator (wasi al-mayyit) and the wasi appointed by the judge (wasi al-qadi). The testator's executor has broader powers because he derives authority directly from the owner's will, whereas the judge's executor is essentially a court-appointed agent with more limited authority. The differences include: (1) Only the testator's executor may contract with himself if beneficial (according to Abu Hanifa); the judge's executor cannot. (2) The judge's executor is bound by specific restrictions placed by the judge. (3) Selling to an inadmissible witness is valid for the testator's executor but not for the judge's. (4) The testator can authorize hiring out the minor for specific trades. (5) The judge cannot dismiss a qualified testator-appointed executor but can dismiss his own appointee. (6) The judge's executor needs fresh permission to take possession after appointment. (7) The judge's prohibition is binding, the testator's is not. (8) Sub-appointment by the judge's executor does not pass authority, unlike the testator's executor, unless the original bequest was general. These rules ensure that the testator's wishes are respected while the judge's appointee remains under judicial control.

Key Terms
وصي الميت (wasi al-mayyit): An executor appointed by the deceased in their will.
وصي القاضي (wasi al-qadi): An executor appointed by the judge when there is no will or the appointed executor fails.
الخلاصة (al-Khulasa): A Hanafi legal work by al-Hasiri, used as a source for the third and sixth differences.
القنية (al-Qunya): A Hanafi fiqh text by al-Farghani, source for the fourth and fifth differences.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a difference between the executor of the deceased and the executor of the judge?
A) Both can sell to themselves if beneficial.
B) The judge can dismiss the executor of the deceased if he is capable.
C) The executor of the judge cannot take possession without fresh permission from the judge.
D) The executor of the deceased cannot hire out the minor for gold embroidery.

Key Takeaway
The judge's executor has eight specific limitations compared to the testator's executor, all stemming from his status as a court-appointed agent rather than an extension of the owner.

Three Cases for Removing a Testator's Executor
Rasm rule limiting qadi's power to remove executor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three cases where a qadi may remove a testator's executor and apply them to a scenario.

Core Idea
A qadi can only remove a testator's executor in three specific, proven cases; general suspicion is insufficient.

The qadi's authority over a testator's executor (wasī) is limited because the executor derives his power from the deceased's choice. This rule from al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair specifies the only grounds for removal, protecting the deceased's will. Understanding this boundary is essential for muftis advising on estate administration.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the three cases
Learn the three grounds: (1) proven treachery (khayanah), (2) deliberately performing a prohibited act, (3) claiming a debt against the deceased but failing to prove it.
These are exhaustive; no other grounds allow removal.
2
Understand the ultimatum in the third case
In the debt claim case, the qadi gives the executor an ultimatum: either release the deceased from the claim or face removal. This allows the executor to retract the claim.
If the executor retracts, he is not removed.
3
Note the condition for appointing a substitute
The qadi cannot appoint another executor while the current one is present, unless he is absent in a way that breaks contact (ghaybah munqati'ah) or acknowledges a debt claimant's right.
This preserves the executor's role as much as possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاضي لا يعزل وصي الميت إلا في ثلاث : فيما إذا ظهرت خيانته، أو تصرف في ما لا يجوز عالمًا مختارًا، أو ادعى دينا على الميت وعجز عن إثباته، ولكن في هذه يقول له : إما أن تبرى الميت أو عزلتك . ولا ينصب وصيا غيره مع وجوده إلا إذا غاب غيبة منقطعة أو أقرَّ لمدَّعي الدين كما في الخزانة.
The qadi does not remove the testator's executor except in three (cases): when his treachery becomes apparent, or he acts in a way that is not permissible knowingly and voluntarily, or he claims a debt against the deceased and fails to prove it. But in this (third case), he says to him: either you declare the deceased free of debt or I will remove you. And he does not appoint another executor in his presence unless he is absent in a way that breaks contact, or he acknowledges (the claim) for the claimant of debt, as in al-Khazanah.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This is a rasm rule that specifies the limited grounds upon which a qadi can remove a testator-appointed executor (wasī al-mayyit). The general principle is that the qadi cannot remove such an executor because he derives authority from the deceased's choice. However, three exceptions: (1) proven treachery (khayanah) – e.g., embezzlement; (2) deliberately performing a prohibited act (e.g., selling property unlawfully); (3) claiming a debt against the testator but failing to prove it – in this case, the qadi gives an ultimatum: either release the deceased from the claim or face removal. Additionally, the qadi does not appoint a co-executor as long as the current executor is present, unless the executor is absent in a way that severs communication (ghaybah munqati'ah) or acknowledges a debt claimant's right. This passage is a memorization anchor for muftis regarding the boundaries of qadi's intervention.

Key Terms
الغيبة المنقطعة (al-ghaybah al-munqaṭi‘ah): An absence where the executor cannot be contacted and no news is received.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which case does the qadi give the executor an ultimatum before removing him?
A) When treachery appears.
B) When he acts impermissibly knowingly.
C) When he claims a debt against the deceased but cannot prove it.
D) When he is absent for a long time.

Key Takeaway
The qadi's power to remove a testator's executor is strictly limited to three cases: treachery, prohibited acts, or unproven debt claims, with an ultimatum in the third case.

The Wasi's Authority and Liability in Hanafi Fiqh
Collection of rulings on wasi's authority and liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the scope of a wasi's authority, identify when liability arises, and apply the rule that the judge cannot interfere while a wasi exists.

Core Idea
The wasi's authority is exclusive; the judge cannot interfere unless the wasi is incapacitated.

This passage presents a series of rulings from the Hanafi school on the powers and liabilities of a wasi (executor). These rules are essential for a mufti to know when advising on estate management and orphan care. The rulings cover when the wasi is liable for mixing funds, releasing debtors, spending on feasts, and the judge's role.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actors and their roles
List the key parties: wasi (executor), judge (qadi), orphan (yatim), debtor (gharim), and guardian (qayyim). Understand their relationships.
The wasi is appointed by the deceased or the judge.
2
Extract the core authorities of the wasi
From the rulings, note that the wasi can execute the will, release an insolvent debtor, and spend on customary feasts. He cannot be removed by the judge unless replaced.
Mixing funds does not automatically impose liability.
3
Identify the limitations on the judge
The judge cannot dispose of orphan property while a wasi exists, even if the judge appointed the wasi. The judge can only appoint a qayyim if the wasi is incapable, but that does not remove the wasi.
This protects the wasi's independence.
4
Note the differences of opinion
On spending for the circumcision feast: some require judge's permission, and some say he is liable absolutely. This is a point of ikhtilaf among Hanafis.
The mufta bihi is the first opinion (no liability if customary).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوصي يملك الإيصاء سواء كان وصي القاضي أو الميت فيها كما في
الوصي إذا خلط مال الصغير بماله لم يضمن منها أيضًا. للوصي إطلاق غريم اليتيم من الحبس إن كان معسرًا لا إن كان موسرًا.
لا يملك القاضي التصرف في مال اليتيم مع وجود وصيه، ولو كان منصوبه كما في بيوع القنية.
لا يضمن الوصي ما أنفقه على وليمة ختان اليتيم إذا كان متعارفًا لا سرف فيه. ومنهم من شرط إذن القاضي وقيل يضمن مطلقا؛ كذا في غصب اليتيمة.
القاضي إذا أقام قيمًا لعجز الوصي لا ينعزل الوصي، وإن أقامه مقام الأول انعزل. كذا في قسمة الولوالجية
إذا مات أحد الوصيين أقام القاضي الحي وصيا أو ضم إليه ،آخر، ولا تبطل إلا إذا أوصى لهما بالتصدّق بالثلث فيضعانه حيث شاءا كذا في الخزانة وفي الثاني خلاف.
The executor has the authority to execute (the will) whether he is the executor of the judge or of the deceased therein, as in
If the executor mixes the property of the minor with his own property, he is not liable for it also. The executor has the authority to release the debtor of the orphan from prison if he is insolvent, not if he is wealthy.
The judge does not have the authority to dispose of the orphan's property while his executor exists, even if he is his appointee, as in Buyu' al-Qunyah.
The executor is not liable for what he spends on the circumcision feast of the orphan if it is customary without extravagance. Some condition the permission of the judge, and it is said he is liable absolutely; likewise in Ghasb al-Yatima.
If the judge appoints a guardian due to the executor's incapacity, the executor is not removed, but if he appoints him in place of the first, he is removed. Likewise in Qismat al-Walwalijiyya.
If one of two executors dies, the judge appoints the living one as executor or attaches another to him, and it does not become void unless they were bequeathed to give charity with the third and they place it wherever they wish, likewise in al-Khizana, and in the second there is disagreement.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage collects several rulings on the powers and liability of the wasi. The wasi has authority to execute the will regardless of who appointed him. If he mixes the minor's property with his own, he is not liable for loss unless negligence is proven. He can release an insolvent debtor of the orphan from prison but not a wealthy one. The judge cannot interfere with the orphan's property while a wasi exists. The wasi is not liable for customary wedding feast expenses for the orphan's circumcision if within reason, though some require judge's permission. If the judge appoints a guardian (qayyim) due to the wasi's incapacity, the wasi is not automatically removed unless replaced. When one of two co-executors dies, the judge can appoint the survivor or add another; the executorship does not become void unless the bequest was specific.

Key Terms
الإيصاء (al-isa): The authority to execute the will and manage the estate.
القيم (al-qayyim): A guardian appointed by the judge to manage the orphan's affairs when the wasi is incapacitated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, if a judge appoints a guardian (qayyim) for the orphan because the wasi is ill, what happens to the wasi's position?
A) The wasi is automatically removed.
B) The wasi is not removed unless the judge replaces him.
C) The wasi must step down immediately.
D) The appointment of a qayyim removes the wasi's authority.

Key Takeaway
The wasi's authority is protected from judge interference; liability arises only from negligence or extreme extravagance, not from mixing or customary spending.

Three Exceptional Rulings: Posthumous Inheritance, Ownership, and Liability
Listing three exceptions where a deceased person can inherit, own, or be liable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three exceptional cases where a deceased person can have legal effects under Hanafi law, and apply the underlying qawida to determine if a new scenario falls within the exceptions.

Core Idea
A deceased cannot inherit, own, or be liable, except in three specific cases.

In general, death ends legal capacity. However, the Hanafi school recognizes three exceptional situations where the deceased's actions before death produce posthumous effects: (1) the fetus inherits the ghurra (blood money) from the one who caused its miscarriage; (2) the deceased can own something in the case of hunting; (3) the deceased can be liable for a wrongfully dug well through his 'aqila. These exceptions ensure that rights are not lost and wrongs are compensated, even after death.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
The default principle: A deceased person does not inherit, own, or incur liability. This is the baseline from which the three exceptions are drawn.
Memorize the rule: 'الميت لا يرث ولا يملك ولا يضمن' before learning the exceptions.
2
Memorize the first exception: inheritance of ghurra
When a person strikes a pregnant woman causing a stillbirth, the ghurra (compensation) is inherited by the fetus from the attacker, and then from the fetus. The right vests in the fetus before its death.
Key: the deceased (attacker) does not inherit; rather, the fetus inherits from the deceased.
3
Memorize the second exception: posthumous ownership
The passage mentions a case from the chapter of hunting where a deceased can own. Though not detailed, it refers to traps or nets set before death that catch game after death; ownership vests in the deceased's estate.
This exception is rarely applied; know it exists.
4
Memorize the third exception: liability for a well
If a person wrongfully digs a well and dies, then someone falls in after death, the blood money is on the 'aqila (paternal relatives). Similarly for a slave who digs, is freed, and dies.
The liability is transferred to the 'aqila because the deceased's estate cannot pay diyah.
5
Identify the common thread
Each exception involves a pre-existing cause (jinayat, hunting, digging) that creates a right or liability surviving death. The 'aqila provides a mechanism for enforcement.
This pattern helps distinguish which post-death acts may be analogized (qiyas is not permitted here).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الميت لا يرث إلا في مسألة ما إذا ضرب بطن امرأة فألقته ميتا فإن الغرة يرثها الجنين لتورث عنه كما في جنايات المبسوط، ولا يملك الميت إلا في مسألة ذكرناها في الصيد، ولا يضمن إلا في مسألة ما إذا حفر بئرا تعديًا ثم مات فوقع فيها إنسان بعد موته كانت الدّية على عاقلته ولو حفر عبد بئرا تعديًا فأعتقه مولاه ثم مات العبد فوقع إنسان فيها فالدية على عاقلته كما في الجامع.
The deceased does not inherit except in one case: when he strikes a woman's belly and she miscarries a stillbirth, then the blood money (ghurra) is inherited by the fetus to be inherited from him, as in the injuries of al-Mabsut. And the deceased does not own except in one case that we mentioned in the chapter of hunting. And he is not liable except in one case: if he dug a well wrongfully and then died, and then a person fell into it after his death, the blood money is upon his 'aqila; and if a slave dug a well wrongfully, then his master emancipated him, then the slave died, and then a person fell into it, the blood money is upon his 'aqila, as in al-Jami'.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage lists three exceptional rulings where a deceased person can have legal consequences: (1) Inheritance: A fetus can inherit the ghurra (blood money) from a person who caused its death, and then that ghurra is inherited from the fetus. (2) Ownership: The deceased can own in one case (presumably hunting, though not detailed here). (3) Liability: If a person wrongfully digs a well and then dies, and someone falls in after his death, the blood money is on his 'aqila (tribal group liable for blood money). Similarly, if a slave digs a well, is freed, then dies, and someone falls in, the blood money is on his 'aqila. These are exceptional to the general rule that the dead have no legal capacity.

Key Terms
غرة (ghurra): Blood money for a fetus that is miscarried due to injury, fixed at a slave or a specific amount.
عاقلة ('aqila): The paternal relatives of the killer who are liable to pay blood money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which case can a deceased person be held liable for blood money according to the passage?
A) If he kills someone before dying
B) If he wrongfully digs a well and later someone falls in after his death
C) If he destroys property after death
D) Never

Key Takeaway
Three exceptional cases where death does not fully extinguish legal capacity: inheritance of ghurra, ownership in hunting, and liability through the 'aqila for a wrongfully dug well.

10 min
Ten Distinctions Between Father and Executor
List of ten legal distinctions between father and executor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply each of the ten legal distinctions between a father and an executor in Hanafi guardianship law.

Core Idea
The father's authority is broader than the executor's in ten specific areas—knowing each is essential for fatwa.

This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir lists ten key differences between the father's guardianship (wilāyah) and the executor's (waṣī). These distinctions govern property transactions, maintenance, marriage, and custody. A mufti must recall these rules when advising on the powers of a guardian after the father's death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule number
Each distinction is numbered (thāniyah = second, thālithah = third, etc.). Note the absence of the first rule (likely on a previous page).
The list starts at the second rule because the first rule is missing from this excerpt.
2
State the legal action
Read the action described: selling/buying, paying debt, eating from wealth, pawning, conducting marriage, etc.
3
Determine which party is allowed
Identify whether the father, the executor, or both can perform the action, and under what condition.
The pattern is often: 'father may do X, but executor may not' or 'both may do X, but with different conditions'.
4
Note the condition or limitation
Each rule includes a qualifier: for the father, 'with the condition that there be no harm'; for the executor, 'with a condition of benevolence' or 'according to his work'.
These conditions reflect the father's inherent authority versus the executor's delegated and restricted role.
5
Synthesize the practical consequence
Formulate a clear statement of the rule that can be used in a fatwa. For example: 'A father may pay his debt from his child's wealth, but an executor cannot.'
The practical consequence is the difference in outcome for the orphan or ward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية : يبيع ويشتري لنفسه بشرط الخيرية لليتيم وللأب ذلك بشرط ألا ضرر . الثالثة : للأب أن يقضي دينه من مال ولده بخلاف الوصي. الرابعة : للأب الأكل من مال ولده عند الحاجة، وللوصي بقدر عمله. الخامسة : للأب أن يرهن مال ولده على دينه بخلاف الوصي. السادسة : لا تقوم عبارته مقام عبارتين فإذا باع أو اشترى لنفسه بالشرط فلا بد من قوله قبلت بعد الإيجاب بخلاف الأب. السابعة : لا يلي الإنكاح بخلاف الأب. الثامنة : لا يمونه بخلاف الأب. التاسعة : لا يؤدي من ماله صدقة فطره بخلاف الأب. العاشرة : لا يستخدمه بخلاف الأب. الحادية عشرة : لا حضانة له بخلاف الأب.
Second: He (the executor) sells and buys for himself with a condition of benevolence for the orphan, while the father may do that with the condition that there be no harm. Third: The father may pay his debt from his child's wealth, unlike the executor. Fourth: The father may eat from his child's wealth when in need, while the executor may take only according to his work. Fifth: The father may pawn his child's wealth for his own debt, unlike the executor. Sixth: His (the executor's) statement does not stand in place of two statements; so when he sells or buys for himself with a condition, he must say 'I accept' after the offer, unlike the father. Seventh: He does not conduct the marriage contract, unlike the father. Eighth: He is not obligated to support (the ward), unlike the father. Ninth: He does not pay the Fitr sadaqah from his own wealth, unlike the father. Tenth: He does not employ (the ward), unlike the father. Eleventh: He has no right of custody, unlike the father.

Commentary
This passage lists eleven specific legal rules that distinguish the powers and duties of a father (أب) from those of an executor (وصي) appointed by the father over minor children. The first rule is missing from this window. The rulings show that the father's authority is broader: he can transact for himself with conditions of benefit, pay his debts from the child's wealth, eat from it when needy, and pawn it for his debt. The executor, by contrast, is more restricted—he must follow the conditions of his appointment, cannot conduct marriage contracts, is not required to support the child from his own wealth, and has no custody rights. These distinctions are essential for a mufti to know when advising on guardianship matters.

Key Terms
وصي (waṣī): An executor appointed by the father to manage the property and affairs of minor children after the father's death.
أب (ab): The natural father, who has full guardianship (wilāyah) over his minor children's property and person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The executor's powers are significantly narrower than the father's in property transactions, marriage, custody, and personal liabilities; a mufti must know these ten distinctions to issue correct rulings.

Estate of a Musta'min: Heirs from Dar al-Harb
Ruling on the estate of a musta'min who dies in Muslim territory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions required for heirs of a deceased musta'min to claim his estate in Dar al-Islam and explain why a royal writ is rejected.

Core Idea
The musta'min's estate is held until heirs prove their claim with testimony, a denial of other heirs, and a surety.

When a non-Muslim with temporary safe conduct (musta'min) dies in Muslim territory, leaving heirs in enemy territory (dar al-harb), the estate cannot be immediately distributed. Islamic law imposes strict safeguards to prevent fraudulent claims. This lesson unpacks the three conditions that must be fulfilled before the heirs can take the estate, as recorded in Fath al-Qadeer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the situation
The musta'min dies in Dar al-Islam and his wealth is in Muslim territory. His heirs are in dar al-harb (enemy territory). The estate is initially withheld (waqf).
The ruling applies only when heirs are in dar al-harb.
2
List the three requirements
When the heirs arrive, they must: (1) provide proof (bayyinah) of their relationship, (2) declare that they know of no other heirs, (3) provide a surety (kafeel).
All three are mandatory.
3
Note what is not accepted
A royal writ (kitab malkihim) from the ruler of dar al-harb is not accepted as evidence, even if authenticated.
Written proof is insufficient; oral testimony is required.
4
Understand the rationale
The surety ensures that if another heir later appears, the surety can be held responsible. The strict proof requirements prevent fraud and protect the estate.
This is a matter of caution in inheritance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو مات المستأمن في دارنا عن مال ورثته في دار الحرب وقف ماله حتى يقدموا فإذا قدموا فلا بدَّ من بينة ولو أهل ذمة ولا بدَّ أن يقولوا لا نعلم له وارثا غيرهم، ويؤخذ منهم كفيل ولا يقبل كتاب ملكهم ولو ثبت أنه كتابه؛ كذا في مستأمن فتح القدير
If a musta'min dies in our land leaving wealth, and his heirs are in dar al-harb, his wealth is withheld until they come. When they come, they must provide proof (bayyinah) even if they are dhimmi people, and they must say 'We do not know of any heir other than us.' A surety (kafeel) is taken from them, and their royal writ is not accepted even if it is proven to be his writ. Thus in the musta'min section of Fath al-Qadeer.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific situation: a musta'min (a non-Muslim given temporary safe conduct) dies in Islamic territory, and his lawful heirs reside in dar al-harb (enemy territory). The estate is not immediately distributed; it is halted (waqf) until the heirs present themselves. When they do, they must: (1) provide evidence of their relationship (bayyinah), (2) swear that they know of no other heir, and (3) provide a surety (kafeel) to ensure the estate is properly claimed. A letter from their ruler is not accepted as proof, even if authenticated. This ruling ensures due process and protects the estate from false claims.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim who enters Muslim territory under a temporary guarantee of safety (amān).
كفيل (kafeel): A surety who guarantees that the claimants are truthful and that no other heirs will appear.
بينة (bayyinah): Clear proof or testimony; in legal context, usually two male witnesses or one male and two females.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When heirs of a deceased musta'min come from dar al-harb to claim his estate, what must they provide?
A) Only a royal writ from their ruler
B) Proof (bayyinah) and a statement that they know of no other heir, and a surety
C) Only proof (bayyinah) without surety
D) A statement that they are the only heirs, no proof needed

Key Takeaway
The musta'min's estate is not automatically given to claimants; it requires proof, a declaration, and a surety, and written authority is rejected.

Agent Not Liable After Agency Terminates
Ruling on agent's liability after termination of agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on agency termination to determine liability when an agent acts in ignorance after the principal's death or discharge.

Core Idea
An agent who acts in ignorance after termination of agency is not liable; the principal or his estate bears the loss.

This lesson examines a critical rule from Jami' al-Fusulayn concerning agency (wakala). When the agency contract ends—by death or discharge—the agent may unknowingly continue acting. The Hanafi school protects such an agent from liability, placing the burden on the principal's side. We will parse the two scenarios given and extract the underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: (1) agent collecting a debt after the principal discharged the debtor, and (2) agent selling a slave after the principal's death. In both, the agent acts without knowing the termination.
Both scenarios involve termination of agency and the agent's ignorance.
2
Determine the agent's knowledge
In each case, the agent is described as 'not knowing' ('لم يعلم'). This ignorance is the key factor that protects him from liability.
If the agent knew, the ruling would differ—he would be liable.
3
State the liability rule
The agent is not liable for the loss of the collected debt or the sale price. The principal (or his estate) bears the loss. In the first case, the principal can seek recourse from the original debtor.
The principal may recover from the debtor in the first scenario; in the second, the estate bears the loss.
4
Analyse the exception
In the first scenario, the principal has the right to demand payment from the debtor because the debtor was discharged but the agent collected. This is a form of recourse, not liability on the agent.
This shows the principal is not left without remedy.
5
Apply to a new case
Consider a principal who becomes insane (terminating agency). If the agent, unaware, continues to act and the proceeds are lost, the agent is not liable. The principal's guardian bears the loss.
The rule applies to any termination, not just death or discharge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي جامع الفصولين وكله بقبض دينه فقبضه بعد إبراء الطالب ولم يعلم فهلك في يده ولم يضمن وللدافع تضمين الموكل، ولو وكله ببيع عبده فباعه بعد موته غير عالم وقبض الثمن وهلك في يده لم يضمن والضمان على الموكل. (انتهى). وأما أحكام الإكراه فمذكورة في آخر المنار، وهي شهيرة في الفروع تركناها قصدا .
In Jami' al-Fusulayn: If someone appointed another to collect his debt, and he collected it after the creditor had discharged the debtor without knowing, and it was destroyed in his hand, he is not liable, and the one who appointed him has the right to hold the principal liable. And if he appointed him to sell his slave, and he sold it after his death without knowing and received the price and it was destroyed in his hand, he is not liable, and the liability is on the principal. (End). As for the rulings on duress, they are mentioned at the end of al-Manar, and they are well-known in the branches; we have deliberately left them out.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage states two rulings from Jami' al-Fusulayn concerning agency after the termination of the agency. First, if the principal (creditor) discharges the debtor without informing the agent, and the agent collects the debt and the money is destroyed in his possession, the agent is not liable because he acted in ignorance. The principal may instead seek recourse from the original debtor (the one who paid). Second, if the principal dies and the agent, unaware, sells the slave and the price is destroyed, again the agent is not liable; the heirs bear the loss. The author notes that rulings on duress are omitted as they are well-known elsewhere.

Key Terms
وكله بقبض دينه (wakkalahu biqabd daynihi): Appointed someone as agent to collect a debt

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an agent sells the principal's slave after the principal's death without knowing, and the price is destroyed, who is liable?
A) The agent
B) The principal's estate
C) The buyer
D) No one

Key Takeaway
An agent who acts in ignorance after termination of agency is not liable; the loss falls on the principal or his estate.
