Rules of Currency Specification (Yata'ayyan) in Transactions
Rulings on currency specification in various transactions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether currency is specified or not in a given transaction and apply the correct Hanafi rule for returning the identical item or its equivalent.

Core Idea
Currency specification depends on transaction type: not in exchange, but yes in trusts and torts.

This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair provides a clear classification for muftis about whether currency (naqd) is considered a specified item. The answer determines if the identical coin must be returned or only its value. This distinction impacts many practical cases, from invalid sales to gifts and trusts.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule for commutative contracts
In sales and other exchange contracts (mu'awadat), currency is not specified. This means if the transaction fails, the equivalent value is owed, not the exact coins.
This is the baseline rule.
2
Analyze void contracts (al-‘aqd al-fasid)
There are two narrations on specification in void contracts. The preferred detail distinguishes: if the contract was void from the start (fasid min aslihi), currency is specified; if it becomes void after being valid, it is not.
The mufta bihi is the detailed distinction.
3
Identify cases where currency is specified
Currency is specified in trusts (amanat), gifts, charity, partnership, mudarabah, and usurpation. Also, in invalid sarf (currency exchange) after invalidity and after destruction of the sold item.
In these cases, the identical item must be returned if it still exists.
4
Apply the rule to dower (mahr) and other special cases
Currency is not specified in dower; if divorce occurs before consummation, the wife returns the equivalent of half the dower, not the exact coins. Zakat on the dower is due on her according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
Also not specified in vows and agency before delivery.
5
Handle shared debt and false claims
In shared debt, the debtor must return half of what he received to his partner. If a claimant admits he had no right, he must return the exact thing he took as long as it remains.
These are cases where specification applies for equitable outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحكام النقد
ما يتعين فيه وما لا يتعيَّن
لا يتعيَّن في المعاوضات وفي تعيينه في العقد الفاسد روايتان، ورجح بعضهم تفصيلاً بأن ما فسد من أصله يتعيَّن فيه لا فيما انتقض بعد صحة، والصحيح تعيينه في الصرف بعد فساده وبعد هلاك المبيع وفي الدين المشترك فيؤمر بردّ نصف ما قبض على شريكه وفيما إذا تبيَّن بطلان القضاء؛ فلو ادَّعى على آخر مالاً وأخذه ثم أقر أنه لم يكن له على خصمه حق فعلى المدعي ردّ عين ما قبض ما دام قائما، ولا يتعيَّن في المهر ولو بعد الطلاق قبل الدخول فترد مثل نصفه ولذا لزمها زكاته لو نصابا حوليا عندهما. ولا يتعيَّن في النذر والوكالة قبل التسليم، وأما بعده فالعامة كذلك، ويتعيَّن في الأمانات والهبة والصدقة والشركة والمضاربة والغصب،
Rulings of currency: What is specified in it and what is not specified. It is not specified in commutative contracts, and regarding its specification in a void contract there are two narrations; some preferred a detail that what is void from its origin is specified in it, not what becomes void after validity. The correct view is its specification in currency exchange after its invalidity and after destruction of the sold item, and in shared debt – he is ordered to return half of what he received to his partner, and in the case where the invalidity of the court judgment becomes clear; so if he claims property from another and takes it, then admits that he had no right against his opponent, the claimant must return the very thing he received as long as it remains. It is not specified in the dower even after divorce before consummation, so she returns the equivalent of half of it, and therefore zakat upon it becomes obligatory on her if it is a nisab for a year according to them. It is not specified in vow and agency before delivery; after delivery, the general ruling is the same; and it is specified in trusts, gifts, charity, partnership, mudarabah, and usurpation.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage outlines the Hanafi rules regarding whether currency (specie) is considered specified (mu'ayyan) or not specified in various legal contexts. In commutative contracts (sales), currency is not specified; if a sale is void, there are two narrations about specification. The preferred view distinguishes between void ab initio (specified) and void after validity (not specified). Currency is specified in invalid currency exchange (sarf) after the transaction becomes void and after the sold item is destroyed. In shared debt, the debtor is ordered to return half of what he took. If a false claim is admitted, the exact thing taken must be returned if it still exists. Dower (mahr) is not specified; if the wife receives the dower and then is divorced before consummation, she must return the equivalent, not the exact coins, and thus zakat is on her. Specification is not required in vows and agency before delivery; after delivery, it remains unspecified. However, currency is specified in trusts (amanat), gifts, charity, partnership, mudarabah, and usurpation (ghasb). This is a detailed classification for muftis to determine rights of return and ownership.

Key Terms
يتعين (yata'ayyan): Becomes specified or determined as a particular item.
المعاوضات (al-mu'āwaḍāt): Commutative contracts (exchange contracts) where each party gives and receives a countervalue.
العقد الفاسد (al-'aqd al-fāsid): A voidable contract that is defective in its essence but may have some legal effects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which of the following is currency NOT specified (la yata'ayyan)?
A) In a trust (amanah)
B) In a gift (hiba)
C) In dower (mahr)
D) In usurpation (ghasb)

Key Takeaway
Currency specification in Hanafi law depends on the legal nature of the transaction: not specified in exchange contracts, but specified in fiduciary relationships and torts.

The Ruling of the Mentally Incapacitated (Ma'tuh)
Ruling on the legal status of the mentally incapacitated

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the ma'tuh, distinguish it from the insane and the minor, and apply the mufta bihi position in fatwa.

Core Idea
The ma'tuh is like a sane minor: his worship is valid but not obligatory.

When issuing a fatwa about a mentally incapacitated person, the mufti must know whether his acts of worship are valid, obligatory, or invalid. This passage from Ibn Nujaym clarifies the Hanafi position by comparing the ma'tuh to three possible categories. Understanding this ruling is essential for correct fatwa in cases of mental impairment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Extract the three rulings mentioned: like a sane minor (valid but not obligatory), like the insane (invalid), like a sane adult (obligatory). Note which is the first mentioned (relied-upon).
The first position is prefaced without 'qil' (it is said), indicating it is the chosen view.
2
Analyze the mufta bihi comparison
Understand why the ma'tuh is compared to a sane minor: he has intermittent sanity, so acts performed with understanding are valid, but he lacks full accountability (taklif) due to deficiency.
The key is balance: not fully insane, not fully competent.
3
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: fatwa is given on the first opinion for ease (taysir). The other opinions are weaker and not acted upon in fatwa.
The mufti must apply the mufta bihi, not the minority views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحكام المعتوه أحكامه أحكام الصبي العاقل فتصح العبادات منه ولا تجب. وقيل هو كالمجنون، وقيل هو كالبالغ العاقل، وقد ذكرناه في النواقص من شرح الكنز .
Rulings of the mentally incapacitated: His rulings are the rulings of the sane minor: acts of worship from him are valid but not obligatory. And it is said he is like the insane, and it is said he is like the sane adult, and we have mentioned it in the chapter of deficiencies from Sharh al-Kanz.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on the ma'tuh (mentally incapacitated person). The author states the relied-upon position: the ma'tuh is analogous to a sane minor (sabi 'aqil) in that his acts of worship are valid (sahih) but not obligatory (ghayr wajib). Then he mentions two other transmissions: one equating him with the insane (majnun) and another equating him with a sane adult (baligh 'aqil). The later reference indicates a fuller treatment elsewhere in Sharh al-Kanz. The mufti should follow the first opinion for fatwa.

Key Terms
المعتوه (al-ma'tuh): A person with a mental deficiency short of insanity; one who has an impaired intellect but not completely insane.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi position on the validity of worship by a ma'tuh?
A) Valid and obligatory
B) Valid but not obligatory
C) Not valid
D) Like the insane, no validity

Key Takeaway
The ma'tuh's worship is valid but not obligatory, and this is the fatwa position for ease.

The Default Ruling: Khunsa Mushkil Treated as Female
Compilation of khunsa rulings with a concluding dabt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default Hanafi ruling for the khunsa mushkil and apply it to at least three fiqh chapters (janaza, salah, faraid).

Core Idea
The khunsa mushkil is presumed female in all rulings unless explicitly excepted.

This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair compiles the scattered rulings on the ambiguous hermaphrodite from earlier Hanafi sources. The key takeaway is a unifying dabt (control rule) that simplifies the mufti's task: the khunsa mushkil is like a female in all rulings, with a limited set of explicit exceptions. This principle guides the mufti when encountering novel scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default principle
Read the final summary: 'وحاصله أنه كالأنثى في جميع الأحكام إلا في مسائل' (The summary: it is like a female in all rulings except in certain matters). This is the overarching rule.
The phrase 'في جميع الأحكام' emphasizes comprehensiveness.
2
Note the type of rule
Recognize that this is a 'dabt' (sub-maxim) within Hanafi qawaid. It is not a kulliya but a restrictive control that applies to a specific category (khunsa mushkil).
Dabt rules often begin with 'الخنثى المشكل كالأنثى' and list exceptions.
3
Apply to janaza (funeral)
From the text: 'ييمم إذا مات ويكفن كفن المرأة' – it is given dry ablution and shrouded in a female shroud. This follows the default: treat as female.
Burial only by a mahram also aligns with female rulings.
4
Apply to salah (prayer)
The text says: 'يقوم أمام النساء خلف الرجال' – it stands in front of women, behind men. This is the male position, but the text elsewhere states it cannot stand in women's row. The default female treatment is modified here, but the principle remains that its prayer is like a female's in some aspects (sits like a woman).
Note: the prayer row ruling is actually an exception (stands in men's row), but the default still applies to other salah rulings.
5
Apply to inheritance (faraid)
The text states: 'إذا مات أبوه فله ميراث أُنثى منه' – it inherits as a female from its father. This is a direct application of the default principle.
This is consistent with the general rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحكام الخنثى المشكل ذكر النسفي في الكنز حقيقته، وذكر من أحكامه وقوفه في الصف وحكم ميراثه وختانه . وذكر مولانا محمد رحمه الله أحكامه في الأصل من كتاب المفقود، وأنا أذكر ما ذكره هناك باختصار . يُيَمَّم إذا مات ويُسجّى قبره ولا يدفنه إلا محرم ويكفن كفن المرأة ولا يلبس حريرا ولا حليًّا في حياته، وإذا قبله رجل بشهوة حرم عليه أصوله وفروعه. فإن زوجه أبوه رجلاً فوصل إليه جاز، وإلا فلا علم لي بذلك، أو امرأة فبلغ فوصل إليها جاز وإلا أُجُل كالعِنّين، ويلبس لباس المرأة في الإحرام ولا يصلي إلا بقناع ويقوم أمام النساء خلف الرجال، وإن وقف في صف النساء أعادها وإن وقف في صف الرجال لا يُعيدها ويُعيدها من عن يمينيه ويساره وخلفه محاذيا له، ويوضع في الجنازة خلف الرجال والمرأة خلفه، ويُجعَل خلف الرجل في القبر لو دفنا لضرورة حاجز بينهما من الصعيد ولا حدّ على قاذفه ولا عليه بقذفه بمنزلة المجبوب؛ وتقطع يده للسرقة ويقطع سارق ماله ويقعد في صلاته كالمرأة ولا قصاص على قاطع يده ولو عمدًا ولو كان القاطع امرأة، ولا تقطع يده إذا قطع يد غيره عمدا، أو على عاقلته أرشها ولا يخلو به رجل ولا امرأة ولا يخلو برجل ولا امرأة، ولا يسافر ثلاثة أيام إلا بمحرم. وإذا أوصى رجل لما في بطن امرأة بألف إن كان غلاما وبخمسمائة إن كان أُنثى، فولدت خنثى مشكلاً فالوصية موقوفة في الخمسمائة الزائدة إلى أن يستبين أمره، ولو قال لامرأته إن كان أول ولد تلدينه غلامًا فأنت طالق أو قال كذلك لأمته فأنت حرة، فولدت خنثى مشكلاً لم تطلق ولم تعتق . ولا سهم له مع المقاتلة وإنما يرضخ له ولا يقتل لو أسيرًا أو مرتدا بعد الإسلام، ولا خراج على رأسه لو كان ذميا، ولا يدخل تحت قول المولى كل عبد لي حرّ أو كل أمة لي حرَّة إلا إذا قالهما فيعتق، ولو قال الزوج إن ملكت عبدا فأنت طالق فاشترى خنثى لم تطلق وكذلك لو قال إن ملكت أمة، ولو قالهما معا طلقت، ولو قال المشكل أنا ذكر أو أنثى لم يقبل قوله، وإذا قتل خطأ وجبت دية المرأة ويوقف الباقي إلى التبيين، وكذا فيما دون النفس، ويصح إعتاقه عن الكفارة، ولو تزوج مشكل مثله لم يجز حتى يتبيَّن فلا يتوارثان بالموت ولو شهد شهود أنه ذكر وشهود أنه أنثى فإن كان يطلب ميرانا قضيت بشهادة من شهد أنه غلام وأبطلت الأخرى، وإن كان رجل يدعي أنه امرأته قضيت بشهادة من شهد أنه أُنثى وأبطلت الأخرى؛ فإن كانت امرأة تدعي أنه زوجها أوقفت الأمر إلى أن يستبين فإن لم يطلب الخنثى شيئًا ولا يطلب منه شيء لا تقبل واحدة منهما حتى يستبين . وأما ميراثه والميراث منه؛ فقال فإن مات أبوه فله ميراث أُنثى منه، وتمامه فيه . وحاصله أنه كالأنثى في جميع الأحكام إلا في مسائل؛ لا يلبس حريرا ولا ذهبًا ولا فضة، ولا يتزوج من رجل، ولا يقف في صف النساء، ولا حد بقذفه، ولا يخلو بامرأة ولا يقع عتق وطلاق علقا على ولادتها أُنثى به، ولا يدخل تحت قوله كل أمة .
Rulings of the ambiguous hermaphrodite: al-Nasafi mentioned in al-Kanz its reality, and mentioned from its rulings: its standing in the row, its inheritance, and its circumcision. And our master Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, mentioned its rulings in al-Asl from the book of the lost person, and I mention what he mentioned there briefly. It is given dry ablution when it dies, its grave is covered, and only a mahram buries it, and it is shrouded in the shroud of a woman. It does not wear silk or jewelry in its life. If a man kisses it with desire, its ascendants and descendants become forbidden to him. If its father marries it to a man and he reaches it, it is valid; otherwise I have no knowledge of that. Or to a woman, and it reaches her, it is valid; otherwise it is given a year like an impotent. It wears the clothing of a woman in ihram and does not pray except with a headcover, and it stands in front of women behind men. If it stands in the row of women, it repeats; if it stands in the row of men, it does not repeat, and it causes those to its right, left, and behind to repeat due to alignment. It is placed in the funeral bier behind men and the woman behind it. In the grave, if buried due to necessity, a barrier of earth is placed between them. There is no hadd on its slanderer, nor on it for slandering; it is like the castrated. Its hand is cut for theft, and the thief of its property is cut. It sits in prayer like a woman. There is no qisas on the one who cuts its hand, even if intentional, even if the cutter is a woman. Its hand is not cut if it cuts the hand of another intentionally; rather the compensation is on its 'aqila. No man or woman may be alone with it, and it may not be alone with a man or woman, nor may it travel three days without a mahram. If a man bequeaths to what is in a woman's womb: a thousand if a boy and five hundred if a girl, and she gives birth to an ambiguous hermaphrodite, the bequest is suspended regarding the extra five hundred until its matter becomes clear. If he said to his wife: 'If your first child is a boy, you are divorced,' or similarly to his slave-girl: 'You are free,' and she gives birth to an ambiguous hermaphrodite, she is not divorced nor freed. It has no share with the fighters, only a discretionary grant is given. It is not killed if taken as a prisoner or an apostate after embracing Islam. No head tax is imposed if it is a dhimmi. It is not included under the statement of the master: 'Every male slave of mine is free' or 'Every female slave of mine is free' unless he says both, then they are freed. If the husband says: 'If I own a male slave, you are divorced,' and he buys a hermaphrodite, she is not divorced; likewise if he says 'female slave'; if he says both together, she is divorced. If the hermaphrodite says 'I am male' or 'I am female', his statement is not accepted. If it kills by mistake, the blood money of a woman is due, and the rest is suspended until clarification; similarly for injuries below the soul. Its emancipation is valid for expiation. If a hermaphrodite marries another hermaphrodite, it is not permissible until clarification; so they do not inherit from each other upon death. If witnesses testify that it is male and others that it is female—if it is seeking inheritance, I decide by the testimony that it is male and nullify the other; if a man claims it is his wife, I decide by the testimony that it is female and nullify the other; if a woman claims it is her husband, I suspend the matter until clarification. If the hermaphrodite seeks nothing nor is anything sought from it, neither testimony is accepted until clarification. As for its inheritance and inheritance from it: he said: if its father dies, it inherits the inheritance of a female from him; the rest is there. The summary: it is like a female in all rulings except in matters: it does not wear silk, gold, or silver, does not marry a man, does not stand in the women's row, no hadd for slandering it, is not alone with a woman, and emancipation and divorce suspended on giving birth to a female are not triggered by it, and it is not included under the statement 'every female slave'.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage compiles the rulings of the khunsa mushkil (ambiguous hermaphrodite) from earlier Hanafi works. The author, likely Ibn Nujaym or a later commentator, summarizes from Imam Muhammad's al-Asl and al-Nasafi's al-Kanz. The rulings cover nearly all fiqh chapters: taharah, janaza, marriage, dress, salah, jihad, hudud, inheritance, and more. The summary at the end provides a key principle: the khunsa mushkil is like a female in all rulings except for a few explicit exceptions. This is a crucial dabt rule for muftis to remember when issuing fatwas regarding such individuals. Note that 'mushkil' means the ambiguity is irresolvable; if gender becomes clear later, the rulings change accordingly.

Key Terms
خنثى مشكل (khunsa mushkil): A person with both male and female genitalia, or no clear gender, where the gender cannot be determined; rulings are based on ambiguity.
رضخ (radhkh): A discretionary grant given to certain persons who do not receive a full share in military spoils, such as women, children, or the khunsa mushkil.
عنين (innin): An impotent man who cannot consummate marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the general principle regarding the khunsa mushkil in Hanafi fiqh?
A) It is treated as male in all rulings.
B) It is treated as female in all rulings except certain listed exceptions.
C) It has a third gender with unique rulings.
D) It is treated as male except in inheritance.

Key Takeaway
The khunsa mushkil is like a female in all Hanafi rulings except the explicit exceptions listed in the text.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
10 min
The Exceptions: Where the Khunsa Differs from the Female Default
List of exceptions to the female default for khunsa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the 7 exceptions to the female default for the khunsa mushkil and explain the rationale behind each.

Core Idea
The khunsa mushkil is female-default, but 7 specific exceptions exist due to doubt in gender.

While the khunsa mushkil is generally treated as a female, the text explicitly lists exceptions where this default is overridden. These exceptions arise from the inherent doubt (shakk) in its gender. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for a mufti to avoid error. In this lesson, we will dissect each exception and its underlying logic.

Guided Path
1
Locate the list of exceptions
In the concluding summary, after stating the default, the author says: 'إلا في مسائل؛ لا يلبس حريرا ولا ذهبًا ولا فضة، ولا يتزوج من رجل، ولا يقف في صف النساء، ولا حد بقذفه، ولا يخلو بامرأة ولا يقع عتق وطلاق علقا على ولادتها أُنثى به، ولا يدخل تحت قوله كل أمة'.
Count them: 7 matters.
2
Understand the first exception: dress (silk, gold, silver)
The khunsa is prohibited from wearing silk, gold, and silver. This is unlike a female, who is permitted. The rationale: these are luxuries generally forbidden to men, and there is doubt that the khunsa might be male.
The prohibition applies in life; at death, it wears a female shroud (which may include silk? No, shroud is simple cloth, not silk).
3
Understand the second exception: marriage to a man
The khunsa cannot be married to a man unless consummation occurs (as per the detailed rule). This is because marriage to a man is only permissible for a biological female; the khunsa's female gender is not certain.
If consummation occurs, it is considered a sign of female gender, so marriage is validated.
4
Understand the third exception: standing in women's row
The khunsa must stand in the men's row, not the women's row. If it stands in the women's row, it must repeat the prayer. Rationale: standing in women's row would expose others to potential fitnah or confusion.
This is an exception to the female-default because the default would place it among women.
5
Understand the fourth exception: no hadd for slander (qadhf)
Slandering the khunsa (accusing it of fornication) does not trigger the hadd punishment. This is like the rule for a female (no hadd for qadhf of a female), but the text lists it as an exception because it might have been expected that since the khunsa is treated as female, slander would still incur hadd? Actually, the rule is that qadhf of a female incurs hadd only if she is free and chaste; for a khunsa, the doubt in gender prevents the full hadd. The text lists it as an exception from the general rule that slander of a person incurs hadd.
The exception here is that the khunsa is not a valid subject for qadhf hadd, similar to a female.
6
Understand the remaining exceptions (five to seven)
Fifth: no khulwa (seclusion) with a woman – the khunsa cannot be alone with a woman, like a man. Sixth: divorce/emancipation conditional on giving birth to a female is not triggered by a khunsa birth. Seventh: the khunsa is not included in the master's statement 'every female slave is free' (because it is not definitively female).
These exceptions are all due to the doubt in gender: the khunsa could be male.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحكام الخنثى المشكل ذكر النسفي في الكنز حقيقته، وذكر من أحكامه وقوفه في الصف وحكم ميراثه وختانه . وذكر مولانا محمد رحمه الله أحكامه في الأصل من كتاب المفقود، وأنا أذكر ما ذكره هناك باختصار . يُيَمَّم إذا مات ويُسجّى قبره ولا يدفنه إلا محرم ويكفن كفن المرأة ولا يلبس حريرا ولا حليًّا في حياته، وإذا قبله رجل بشهوة حرم عليه أصوله وفروعه. فإن زوجه أبوه رجلاً فوصل إليه جاز، وإلا فلا علم لي بذلك، أو على رأسه لو كان ذميا، ولا يدخل تحت قول المولى كل عبد لي حرّ أو كل أمة لي حرَّة إلا إذا قالهما فيعتق، ولو قال الزوج إن ملكت عبدا فأنت طالق فاشترى خنثى لم تطلق وكذلك لو قال إن ملكت أمة، ولو قالهما معا طلقت، ولو قال المشكل أنا ذكر أو أنثى لم يقبل قوله، وإذا قتل خطأ وجبت دية المرأة ويوقف الباقي إلى التبيين، وكذا فيما دون النفس، ويصح إعتاقه عن الكفارة، ولو تزوج مشكل مثله لم يجز حتى يتبيَّن فلا يتوارثان بالموت ولو شهد شهود أنه ذكر وشهود أنه أنثى فإن كان يطلب ميرانا قضيت بشهادة من شهد أنه غلام وأبطلت الأخرى، وإن كان رجل يدعي أنه امرأته قضيت بشهادة من شهد أنه أُنثى وأبطلت الأخرى؛ فإن كانت امرأة تدعي أنه زوجها أوقفت الأمر إلى أن يستبين فإن لم يطلب الخنثى شيئًا ولا يطلب منه شيء لا تقبل واحدة منهما حتى يستبين . وأما ميراثه والميراث منه؛ فقال فإن مات أبوه فله ميراث أُنثى منه، وتمامه فيه . وحاصله أنه كالأنثى في جميع الأحكام إلا في مسائل؛ لا يلبس حريرا ولا ذهبًا ولا فضة، ولا يتزوج من رجل، ولا يقف في صف النساء، ولا حد بقذفه، ولا يخلو بامرأة ولا يقع عتق وطلاق علقا على ولادتها أُنثى به، ولا يدخل تحت قوله كل أمة .
Rulings of the ambiguous hermaphrodite: al-Nasafi mentioned in al-Kanz its reality, and mentioned from its rulings: its standing in the row, its inheritance, and its circumcision. And our master Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, mentioned its rulings in al-Asl from the book of the lost person, and I mention what he mentioned there briefly. It is given dry ablution when it dies, its grave is covered, and only a mahram buries it, and it is shrouded in the shroud of a woman. It does not wear silk or jewelry in its life. If a man kisses it with desire, its ascendants and descendants become forbidden to him. If its father marries it to a man and he reaches it, it is valid; otherwise I have no knowledge of that. Or to a woman, and it reaches her, it is valid; otherwise it is given a year like an impotent. It wears the clothing of a woman in ihram and does not pray except with a headcover, and it stands in front of women behind men. If it stands in the row of women, it repeats; if it stands in the row of men, it does not repeat, and it causes those to its right, left, and behind to repeat due to alignment. It is placed in the funeral bier behind men and the woman behind it. In the grave, if buried due to necessity, a barrier of earth is placed between them. There is no hadd on its slanderer, nor on it for slandering; it is like the castrated. Its hand is cut for theft, and the thief of its property is cut. It sits in prayer like a woman. There is no qisas on the one who cuts its hand, even if intentional, even if the cutter is a woman. Its hand is not cut if it cuts the hand of another intentionally; rather the compensation is on its 'aqila. No man or woman may be alone with it, and it may not be alone with a man or woman, nor may it travel three days without a mahram. If a man bequeaths to what is in a woman's womb: a thousand if a boy and five hundred if a girl, and she gives birth to an ambiguous hermaphrodite, the bequest is suspended regarding the extra five hundred until its matter becomes clear. If he said to his wife: 'If your first child is a boy, you are divorced,' or similarly to his slave-girl: 'You are free,' and she gives birth to an ambiguous hermaphrodite, she is not divorced nor freed. It has no share with the fighters, only a discretionary grant is given. It is not killed if taken as a prisoner or an apostate after embracing Islam. No head tax is imposed if it is a dhimmi. It is not included under the statement of the master: 'Every male slave of mine is free' or 'Every female slave of mine is free' unless he says both, then they are freed. If the husband says: 'If I own a male slave, you are divorced,' and he buys a hermaphrodite, she is not divorced; likewise if he says 'female slave'; if he says both together, she is divorced. If the hermaphrodite says 'I am male' or 'I am female', his statement is not accepted. If it kills by mistake, the blood money of a woman is due, and the rest is suspended until clarification; similarly for injuries below the soul. Its emancipation is valid for expiation. If a hermaphrodite marries another hermaphrodite, it is not permissible until clarification; so they do not inherit from each other upon death. If witnesses testify that it is male and others that it is female—if it is seeking inheritance, I decide by the testimony that it is male and nullify the other; if a man claims it is his wife, I decide by the testimony that it is female and nullify the other; if a woman claims it is her husband, I suspend the matter until clarification. If the hermaphrodite seeks nothing nor is anything sought from it, neither testimony is accepted until clarification. As for its inheritance and inheritance from it: he said: if its father dies, it inherits the inheritance of a female from him; the rest is there. The summary: it is like a female in all rulings except in matters: it does not wear silk, gold, or silver, does not marry a man, does not stand in the women's row, no hadd for slandering it, is not alone with a woman, and emancipation and divorce suspended on giving birth to a female are not triggered by it, and it is not included under the statement 'every female slave'.

Note that 'mushkil' means the ambiguity is irresolvable; if gender becomes clear later, the rulings change accordingly.

Key Terms
خنثى مشكل (khunsa mushkil): A person with both male and female genitalia, or no clear gender, where the gender cannot be determined; rulings are based on ambiguity.
رضخ (radhkh): A discretionary grant given to certain persons who do not receive a full share in military spoils, such as women, children, or the khunsa mushkil.
عنين (innin): An impotent man who cannot consummate marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many exceptions are listed in the concluding summary of the khunsa rulings?
A) 5
B) 6
C) 7
D) 8

Key Takeaway
There are exactly seven exceptions to the female-default ruling for the khunsa mushkil, arising from the doubt in its gender.

Rulings of the Female: Grooming and Purification
First part of the list of female-specific rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on female circumcision, pubic hair removal, beard shaving, head shaving, and semen purification for women, and identify the mufta bihi positions.

Core Idea
For women, circumcision is an honor, not a sunnah; and semen is not purified by rubbing.

This lesson covers the first part of the text on female-specific rulings. The Hanafi school distinguishes between male and female in grooming and purification matters. The mufti must know these differences to issue correct fatwas, especially on female circumcision and purification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
For each item (pubic hair, circumcision, beard, head shaving, semen purification), locate the act and the explicit legal classification (sunnah, mubah, makruh, etc.).
2
Analyze the type of ruling
Determine if the ruling is sunnah, mukramah, or makruh, and note any conditions (e.g., 'on one opinion').
Mukramah is a Hanafi-specific category meaning honorable but not legally recommended.
3
Check for multiple opinions
Some rulings have alternative views (e.g., semen purification). Identify the mufta bihi (relied) position.
If the text says 'ala qawl', the opposed view is also known.

Rulings of the Female: Awrah and Prayer
Second part of the list: awrah, prayer differences, and congregational rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the relied-upon awrah of a woman in Hanafi fiqh, explain the rules for women in prayer (raising hands, recitation, posture, alerting imam), and state the ruling on women's congregation and imama.

Core Idea
A woman's entire body is awrah except face, hands, and feet; she claps in prayer instead of saying subhanallah.

This lesson continues the text's enumeration of female-specific rulings, focusing on awrah and prayer-related differences. The mufti must be aware of multiple opinions on awrah and the practical adjustments women make in salah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the awrah rulings
List the three views: relied (face, hands, feet exposed), weaker (forearms also), alternative (voice is awrah).
The relied view is the one marked 'ala al-mu'tamad'.
2
Analyze prayer gestures
Note the differences: not raising hands to ears, not reciting aloud, drawing together in ruku' and sujud, not spreading fingers.
These emphasize modesty and concealment.
3
Understand alerting the imam
Women clap rather than say 'subhanallah' so as not to be heard.
This is to avoid fitna.
4
Examine congregational rules
Women's jama'ah is disliked; if done, the imam stands in the middle; woman cannot be imam for men.

Source Text

عورة (awrah): Parts of the body that must be covered in prayer and in front of non-mahram.

Women as Prophets and Exemption from Sultanic Fines
Ruling on women's prophethood and fine exemption as rasm al-mufti

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi ruling on women's prophethood and apply the principle of exemption from sultanic fines in fatwa practice.

Core Idea
Women can be prophets but not messengers; they are also exempt from sultanic fines.

This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir presents a rasm al-mufti rule: the Hanafi position that women can attain prophethood but not messengership, and a separate ruling that women are not subject to state-imposed financial penalties. These rulings illustrate how the mufti must distinguish between categories of revelation and apply gender-based exemptions in financial matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two distinct rulings
The passage contains two separate legal statements: one about women's prophethood/messengership and one about their exemption from sultanic fines. Note which part belongs to which ruling.
The phrase 'ولا تدخل النساء في الغرامات السلطانية' is a separate ruling from the prophethood discussion.
2
Analyze the reasoning for prophethood vs messengership
The author uses 'li-anna al-risala mabniyya 'ala al-ishtihar' (messengership is built on fame) and 'mabna halihinna 'ala al-satr' (their condition is built on concealment). Understand how this distinction leads to the Hanafi position.
This is a rational (aqli) argument based on the nature of women and the requirements of messengership.
3
Determine the authority level for the fine exemption
The passage cites 'al-Walwalijiyya from the section on division'. Identify this as a source within the Hanafi tradition and assess its authority level (zahir al-riwaya, nawadir, or fatawa mutakhkhirin).
Al-Walwalijiyya is a later Hanafi work by al-Walwaliji (d. 540 AH), so this is likely from the fatawa mutakhkhirin category.
4
Formulate the fatwa hierarchy
State the mufta bihi position for both rulings. For prophethood: the chosen view is that women can be prophets but not messengers. For fines: women are exempt. Determine which authority (zahir al-riwaya or later fatwa) supports each.
The prophethood view comes from al-Musayara (a later work), while the fine exemption is from al-Walwalijiyya.
5
Apply to a modern nawazil scenario
Consider a modern case: a female scholar claims to receive revelation. Use the rasm al-mufti to guide whether this is possible (prophethood) or impossible (messengership). Also, apply the fine exemption to a state penalty imposed on a woman.
The ruling on fines remains applicable in contemporary contexts where states impose financial penalties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في جواز كونها نبيَّة واختار في المسايرة جواز كونها نبيَّة لا رسولة، لأن الرسالة مبنية على الاشتهار ومبنى حالهنَّ على الستر بخلاف النبوة والتمام فيها، ولا تدخل النساء في الغرامات السلطانية كما في الولوالجية من القسمة .
And they differed regarding the permissibility of her being a prophet. And [the author] chose in al-Musayara the permissibility of her being a prophet, not a messenger, because messengership is based on fame and their condition is based on concealment, unlike prophethood. And the completion is in it. And women are not included in sultanic fines, as in al-Walwalijiyya from the section on division.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on the status of women regarding prophethood. The author states that there is a difference of opinion on whether a woman can be a prophet. He adopts the view from al-Musayara that a woman can be a prophet but not a messenger. The reasoning is that a messenger requires public recognition and fame (ishtihar), while women's nature is based on concealment (satr). Prophethood does not require such fame. Then the passage adds that women are not subject to sultanic fines (gharamat sultaniyya), citing al-Walwalijiyya. This is a separate ruling, indicating that women are exempt from certain state-imposed financial penalties.

Key Terms
نبي (nabiyy): Prophet; one who receives revelation but is not necessarily commanded to convey it publicly.
رسول (rasul): Messenger; one who is commanded to convey revelation publicly.
غرامات سلطانية (gharamat sultaniyya): Fines imposed by the ruler or state authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for allowing women to be prophets but not messengers?
A) Prophethood requires public fame, which women lack.
B) Messengership requires public fame, while prophethood does not.
C) Women are naturally more suited for prophethood.
D) The Qur'an explicitly prohibits women from being messengers.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that women can be prophets (due to no requirement of public fame) but cannot be messengers, and they are exempt from sultanic fines.

Worship and Purification of the Dhimmi
Worship and purification rulings for the dhimmi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on the validity of a dhimmi's purification acts and the invalidity of his worship, and explain the consequence upon conversion.

Core Idea
The dhimmi's worship is invalid, but his wudu and ghusl are valid and ready for use if he accepts Islam.

This lesson examines the legal treatment of a dhimmi in matters of worship and purification. While he is not obligated to perform acts of worship and they are not accepted from him, his wudu and ghusl are considered valid. This distinction is crucial for muftis when dealing with conversions.

Guided Path
1
State the general principle
Identify the baseline: the dhimmi is like a Muslim in civil matters but differs in worship.
2
List the worship rulings
Note: not commanded to worship, worship is invalid, tayammum invalid, but wudu and ghusl valid.
Tayammum requires intention (niyyah) which a non-Muslim cannot validly have.
3
Understand the sin rulings
He does not sin for abandoning worship according to one opinion, but sins for abandoning belief by consensus.
This indicates a slight ikhtilaf within the school.
4
Apply the conversion scenario
If he becomes Muslim, his prayer performed with that valid wudu is valid.
The wudu is a condition; the prayer becomes valid upon iman.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحكام الذمّي
له حكمه حكم المسلمين؛ إلا أنه لا يؤمر بالعبادات ولا تصح منه ولا يصح تيممه، ويصح وضوؤه وغسله فلو أسلم جازت صلاته به. ولا يأثم على ترك العبادات على قول، ويأثم على ترك اعتقادها إجماعا ولا يمنع من دخول المسجد جُنُبًا بخلاف المسلم ولا يتوقف جواز دخوله على إذن مسلم عندنا، ولو كان المسجد الحرام. ولا يصح نذره ولا سهم من الغنيمة. ويرضخ له إن قاتل أو دل على الطريق، ولا يحدّ بشرب الخمر ولا تُراق عليه بل تردّ عليه إذا غصبت منه . ويضمن متلفها له إلا أن يظهر بيعها بين المسلمين فلا ضمان في إراقتها أو يكون المتلف إماما يرى ذلك بخلاف إتلاف خمر المسلم فإنه لا يوجب الضمان ولو كان المتلف ذميًا، وينبغي أن يكون إظهاره شربها كإظهاره بيعها ولم أره الآن، ولا يمنع من لبس الحرير والذهب ولا يعترض لهم لو تناكحوا فاسدا أو تبايعوا كذلك ثم أسلموا.
Rulings on the dhimmi: He has the same ruling as Muslims, except that he is not commanded to perform acts of worship, and they are not valid from him, and his tayammum is not valid, but his wudu and ghusl are valid. If he becomes Muslim, his prayer is valid with it. And he does not sin for abandoning worship according to one opinion, but he sins for abandoning belief in them by consensus. And he is not prevented from entering the mosque in a state of janabah, unlike a Muslim. And his entry is not conditional on the permission of a Muslim according to us, even if it is the Sacred Mosque. And his vow is not valid, nor does he receive a share of the spoils. He is given a portion if he fights or guides on the road. And he is not punished by hadd for drinking wine, nor is his wine poured out, but it is returned to him if it is usurped from him. And the one who destroys it must guarantee it, unless he appears to sell it among Muslims, then there is no guarantee for pouring it, or if the destroyer is an imam who holds that view. Unlike destroying the wine of a Muslim, which does not necessitate guarantee, even if the destroyer is a dhimmi. And it should be that his manifesting drinking it is like his manifesting selling it, but I have not seen it now. And he is not prevented from wearing silk and gold, and they are not objected to if they marry invalidly or trade invalidly and then become Muslim.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage covers many rulings concerning the dhimmi (non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state). It begins by stating that the dhimmi has the same legal rulings as Muslims in civil matters, with exceptions. He is not obligated to perform acts of worship like prayer, fasting, etc., and if he performs them, they are not valid because he lacks the prerequisite of faith. However, his wudu and ghusl are valid, so if he converts, his prayer performed with that wudu is valid. He is not sinful for omitting worship according to one opinion, but he is sinful for omitting belief in them by consensus. He may enter the mosque even in a state of janabah, unlike a Muslim, and no permission is needed, even for the Sacred Mosque. His nadhr (vow) is invalid. He receives no share of the spoils of war, but may get a portion if he fights or guides. The hadd penalty for drinking wine is not applied to him, and his wine is not to be poured out; if it is taken, it must be returned. If someone destroys it, they must guarantee it unless the dhimmi openly sells it among Muslims, or if the destroyer is an imam who permits wine. Destroying a Muslim's wine does not incur guarantee. He is allowed to wear silk and gold. Invalid marriages and transactions among them are not interfered with initially, but if they become Muslim, those contracts are considered void and need to be rectified.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim permanent resident of the Islamic state under protection.
تيمم (tayammum): Dry ablution with pure earth in absence of water or due to inability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is true regarding a dhimmi's wudu?
A) His wudu is valid
B) His wudu is invalid
C) His wudu is only valid if he intends to become Muslim
D) He cannot perform wudu

Key Takeaway
A dhimmi's purification acts are valid in themselves, but his worship requires faith for acceptance.

Mosque Entry and Vows of the Dhimmi
Rulings on dhimmi entering mosques and validity of vows

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on a dhimmi entering the mosque (including in janabah and without permission) and the invalidity of his vow.

Core Idea
A dhimmi may enter any mosque even in janabah and without permission; his vow (nadhr) is invalid.

This lesson covers two distinct rulings regarding the dhimmi: his ability to enter mosques, including the Sacred Mosque, even in a state of major impurity, and the legal status of his vows. These rules reflect the Hanafi view that the mosque is open to all, subject to certain conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mosque entry rules
The dhimmi is not prevented from entering the mosque even if in janabah, unlike a Muslim. No permission from a Muslim is required, even for the Sacred Mosque.
2
Compare with Muslim ruling
A Muslim in janabah is prohibited from staying in the mosque; the dhimmi is allowed.
This is because janabah is a spiritual impurity that only affects Muslims.
3
Address the vow ruling
The dhimmi's vow (nadhr) is not valid because he lacks the legal capacity to make an offering to Allah.
Vow is an act of worship requiring faith.
4
Synthesize the two rules
Both rulings stem from the same principle: the dhimmi is not obligated in worship, so acts like janabah restrictions and vows are not applicable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Rulings on the dhimmi: He has the same ruling as Muslims, except that he is not commanded to perform acts of worship, and they are not valid from him, and his tayammum is not valid, but his wudu and ghusl are valid. If he becomes Muslim, his prayer is valid with it. And he does not sin for abandoning worship according to one opinion, but he sins for abandoning belief in them by consensus. And he is not prevented from entering the mosque in a state of janabah, unlike a Muslim. And his entry is not conditional on the permission of a Muslim according to us, even if it is the Sacred Mosque. And his vow is not valid, nor does he receive a share of the spoils. He is given a portion if he fights or guides on the road. And he is not punished by hadd for drinking wine, nor is his wine poured out, but it is returned to him if it is usurped from him. And the one who destroys it must guarantee it, unless he appears to sell it among Muslims, then there is no guarantee for pouring it, or if the destroyer is an imam who holds that view. Unlike destroying the wine of a Muslim, which does not necessitate guarantee, even if the destroyer is a dhimmi. And it should be that his manifesting drinking it is like his manifesting selling it, but I have not seen it now. And he is not prevented from wearing silk and gold, and they are not objected to if they marry invalidly or trade invalidly and then become Muslim.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage covers many rulings concerning the dhimmi (non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state). It begins by stating that the dhimmi has the same legal rulings as Muslims in civil matters, with exceptions. He is not obligated to perform acts of worship like prayer, fasting, etc., and if he performs them, they are not valid because he lacks the prerequisite of faith. However, his wudu and ghusl are valid, so if he converts, his prayer performed with that wudu is valid. He is not sinful for omitting worship according to one opinion, but he is sinful for omitting belief in them by consensus. He may enter the mosque even in a state of janabah, unlike a Muslim, and no permission is needed, even for the Sacred Mosque. His nadhr (vow) is invalid. He receives no share of the spoils of war, but may get a portion if he fights or guides. The hadd penalty for drinking wine is not applied to him, and his wine is not to be poured out; if it is taken, it must be returned. If someone destroys it, they must guarantee it unless the dhimmi openly sells it among Muslims, or if the destroyer is an imam who permits wine. Destroying a Muslim's wine does not incur guarantee. He is allowed to wear silk and gold. Invalid marriages and transactions among them are not interfered with initially, but if they become Muslim, those contracts are considered void and need to be rectified.

Key Terms
جنب (junub): State of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl.
نذر (nadhr): A vow to perform an act of worship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi law, may a dhimmi enter the Sacred Mosque without permission?
A) Yes, he may enter without permission.
B) No, he needs permission from a Muslim.
C) Yes, but only if he is not in janabah.
D) No, it is prohibited for non-Muslims.

Key Takeaway
A dhimmi is not bound by worship-related restrictions such as janabah prohibitions and his vows have no legal effect.

Wine, Spoils, Silk, Gold, and Invalid Contracts of the Dhimmi
Rulings on spoils, wine, silk/gold, and invalid contracts for dhimmi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on: dhimmi's share of spoils, hadd for wine, guarantee for wine, permissibility of silk/gold, and effect of invalid contracts upon conversion.

Core Idea
The dhimmi is not subject to hadd for wine, his wine is guaranteed if destroyed unless sold openly, and invalid contracts are not intercepted until conversion.

This lesson covers a set of rulings on the dhimmi's relation to wine, spoils of war, personal adornment, and contractual disputes. These rules reflect the principle that the dhimmi's personal matters are left undisturbed until they affect Muslims or until he converts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the spoils ruling
The dhimmi receives no share of spoils but may be given a portion (yurdakh lahu) if he fights or guides.
2
Examine the wine rulings
No hadd for drinking; wine is not to be poured out; if usurped, it must be returned; destruction guarantees compensation unless sold openly or destroyer is an imam who permits wine.
Contrast with Muslim's wine: destroying it incurs no guarantee.
3
Note the silk/gold ruling
The dhimmi is not prevented from wearing silk and gold; these are restricted for Muslim men but allowed for dhimmi men.
4
Address invalid contracts
Dhimmis are not interfered with if they contract invalid marriages or sales; but upon conversion, these contracts must be rectified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحكام الذمّي
له حكمه حكم المسلمين؛ إلا أنه لا يؤمر بالعبادات ولا تصح منه ولا يصح تيممه، ويصح وضوؤه وغسله فلو أسلم جازت صلاته به. ولا يأثم على ترك العبادات على قول، ويأثم على ترك اعتقادها إجماعا ولا يمنع من دخول المسجد جُنُبًا بخلاف المسلم ولا يتوقف جواز دخوله على إذن مسلم عندنا، ولو كان المسجد الحرام. ولا يصح نذره ولا سهم من الغنيمة. ويرضخ له إن قاتل أو دل على الطريق، ولا يحدّ بشرب الخمر ولا تُراق عليه بل تردّ عليه إذا غصبت منه . ويضمن متلفها له إلا أن يظهر بيعها بين المسلمين فلا ضمان في إراقتها أو يكون المتلف إماما يرى ذلك بخلاف إتلاف خمر المسلم فإنه لا يوجب الضمان ولو كان المتلف ذميًا، وينبغي أن يكون إظهاره شربها كإظهاره بيعها ولم أره الآن، ولا يمنع من لبس الحرير والذهب ولا يعترض لهم لو تناكحوا فاسدا أو تبايعوا كذلك ثم أسلموا.
Rulings on the dhimmi: He has the same ruling as Muslims, except that he is not commanded to perform acts of worship, and they are not valid from him, and his tayammum is not valid, but his wudu and ghusl are valid. If he becomes Muslim, his prayer is valid with it. And he does not sin for abandoning worship according to one opinion, but he sins for abandoning belief in them by consensus. And he is not prevented from entering the mosque in a state of janabah, unlike a Muslim. And his entry is not conditional on the permission of a Muslim according to us, even if it is the Sacred Mosque. And his vow is not valid, nor does he receive a share of the spoils. He is given a portion if he fights or guides on the road. And he is not punished by hadd for drinking wine, nor is his wine poured out, but it is returned to him if it is usurped from him. And the one who destroys it must guarantee it, unless he appears to sell it among Muslims, then there is no guarantee for pouring it, or if the destroyer is an imam who holds that view. Unlike destroying the wine of a Muslim, which does not necessitate guarantee, even if the destroyer is a dhimmi. And it should be that his manifesting drinking it is like his manifesting selling it, but I have not seen it now. And he is not prevented from wearing silk and gold, and they are not objected to if they marry invalidly or trade invalidly and then become Muslim.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage covers many rulings concerning the dhimmi (non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state). It begins by stating that the dhimmi has the same legal rulings as Muslims in civil matters, with exceptions. He is not obligated to perform acts of worship like prayer, fasting, etc., and if he performs them, they are not valid because he lacks the prerequisite of faith. However, his wudu and ghusl are valid, so if he converts, his prayer performed with that wudu is valid. He is not sinful for omitting worship according to one opinion, but he is sinful for omitting belief in them by consensus. He may enter the mosque even in a state of janabah, unlike a Muslim, and no permission is needed, even for the Sacred Mosque. His nadhr (vow) is invalid. He receives no share of the spoils of war, but may get a portion if he fights or guides. The hadd penalty for drinking wine is not applied to him, and his wine is not to be poured out; if it is taken, it must be returned. If someone destroys it, they must guarantee it unless the dhimmi openly sells it among Muslims, or if the destroyer is an imam who permits wine. Destroying a Muslim's wine does not incur guarantee. He is allowed to wear silk and gold. Invalid marriages and transactions among them are not interfered with initially, but if they become Muslim, those contracts are considered void and need to be rectified.

Key Terms
رضخ (yurdakh): A discretionary portion given to a combatant who is not entitled to a full share.
حد (hadd): Fixed Quranic punishment for specific crimes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A Muslim destroys a dhimmi's wine that the dhimmi was openly selling to Muslims. According to the text, does the Muslim have to guarantee it?
A) Yes, because wine is his property.
B) No, because the dhimmi openly sold it among Muslims.
C) Yes, because destroying property always requires guarantee.
D) No, because wine is impure.

Key Takeaway
A dhimmi's wine is protected property, he is not liable for hadd, and his personal contracts are not interfered with until conversion.

Accepting a Dhimmi's Statement in Mu'amalat
Rasm rule on accepting a dhimmi's statement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rasm rule that a dhimmi's statement on halal and haram is accepted only within transactions (mu'amalat), and distinguish this from primary religious rulings.

Core Idea
A dhimmi's word on halal/haram is accepted only within transactions, not as a primary ruling.

This lesson examines a nuanced rasm rule: can a mufti rely on a dhimmi's statement regarding permissibility and prohibition? The text from al-Kanz initially suggests absolute acceptance, but al-Zayla'i objects. The resolution clarifies that acceptance is restricted to transactions, not core religious matters. Understanding this distinction is crucial for proper fatwa practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling in al-Kanz
Locate the claim: 'The statement of a kafir is accepted regarding what is permissible and prohibited.' Note this is a general statement.
This appears in a rasm al-mufti context.
2
Recognize al-Zayla'i's Objection
Al-Zayla'i calls it a mistake and says the statement is not accepted at all. This creates a dispute.
Understand that ijma' on the rule is not absolute.
3
Analyze the Reconciliation
The response limits acceptance to transactions (mu'amalat), not as a primary matter. This is the relied-upon view.
The purpose is to clarify the intended scope.
4
Apply the Rule to a Scenario
Practice: if a dhimmi says a certain meat is permissible, can you accept it? Yes, if it's a business context; no, if it's a matter of worship (e.g., halal slaughter).
The key is the context of the statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الكنز : ويقبل قول الكافر في الحل والحرمة. وتعقبه الزيلعي بأنه سهو ولا يقبل قوله فيهما وجوابه أنه يقبل فيهما ضمن المعاملات لا مقصودا وهو مراده كما أفصح به في الكافي.
And in al-Kanz: The statement of a kafir is accepted regarding what is permissible and prohibited. Al-Zayla'i criticized it, saying it is a mistake, and that his statement is not accepted in them. And the answer is that it is accepted in the context of transactions, not as a primary matter, and that is his intended meaning, as clarified in al-Kafi.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute about whether a dhimmi's statement can be accepted regarding halal and haram. According to al-Kanz, it is accepted. Al-Zayla'i objects, saying this is an error. The response reconciles by limiting acceptance to matters of transactions (mu'amalat), not in primary matters of belief or law. The author of al-Kafi clarifies this nuance. Thus, if a dhimmi says something is permissible in a business context, it may be relied upon, but not for fundamental religious rulings.

Key Terms
حل وحرمة (halal wa hurma): Permissibility and prohibition.
ضمن المعاملات (dimn al-mu'amalat): Within the context of transactions or dealings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, when is a dhimmi's statement on halal and haram accepted?
A) In all matters
B) In matters of worship
C) In matters of transactions
D) Never

Key Takeaway
A dhimmi's statement on halal and haram is accepted in transactional contexts but not for primary religious rulings.

Distinguishing Dhimmis in Dress and Residence
Rulings on outward distinctions for dhimmis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on distinguishing dhimmis in dress, mount, residence, and worship places, and identify the relied-upon positions when narrations differ.

Core Idea
Dhimmis must be outwardly distinguishable from Muslims to prevent imitation and preserve Islamic identity.

This passage from Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir presents a set of regulatory rules for dhimmis regarding their appearance, mounts, and dwellings. These rules are part of the mufti's toolset for advising on public order and Islamic identity. The text also records internal Hanafi differences on certain points, indicating the need for careful tarjih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories of distinction
The passage covers: mount, dress, house marking, place of worship, residence location, and behavior in public. List each category.
Use the Arabic terms: مركب, ملبس, علامة على الدور, بيعة/كنيسة, سكنى.
2
State the basic rule for each category
For each category, extract the general requirement: e.g., ride on pack-saddles, do not wear tayalisah/ardiya, place a sign on houses, no new churches in misr.
The rules are explicitly mentioned; note the details.
3
Note the disagreements and their resolved positions
There are two ikhtilafs: residence (with Muslims vs separate quarter) and number of signs (all or one). State each side and the mufta bihi (المعتمد).
The text says 'والمعتمد الجواز في محلة خاصة' and 'والمعتمد أنهم لا يركبون مطلقا ولا يلبسون العمائم'.
4
Extract the mufta bihi (relied-upon) rule
Synthesize the final position: they do not ride at all, do not wear turbans, place a house sign, do not build new churches, reside in a separate quarter, and if riding a donkey out of necessity, dismount in crowds and pass through narrow ways.
This is the operative fatwa.
5
Consider the rationale (illah)
The purpose is tamyiz (distinction) to avoid confusion and protect Islamic symbols. Reflect on how each rule serves this purpose.
Understanding the illah aids in applying the rule to new situations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويأخذ الذمّي بالتميّز عنا في المركب والملبس فيركبون بالأكف ولا يلبسون الطيالسة والأردية ولا ثياب أهل العلم والشرف وتُجعَل على دورهم علامة، ولا يحدثون بيعة ولا كنيسة في مصر. واختلفت الرواية في سكناهم بين المسلمين في المصر، والمعتمد الجواز في محلة خاصة واختلف المشايخ رحمهم الله هل يلزم تمييزهم بجميع العلامات أو تكفي واحدة والمعتمد أنهم لا يركبون مطلقا ولا يلبسون العمائم. وإن ركب الحمار لضرورة نزل في المجامع ويضيق عليه في المرور.
And the dhimmi is taken to be distinguished from us in mount and dress: they ride on pack-saddles (akf), and they do not wear tayalisah (scarves) and ardiya (cloaks), nor the clothes of scholars and nobles. And a sign is placed on their houses. And they do not build a church or synagogue in a city (misr). And the narration differed regarding their residence among Muslims in the city, and the relied-upon view is that it is permissible in a specific quarter. And the scholars differed whether it is necessary to distinguish them with all the signs or one is sufficient, and the relied-upon view is that they do not ride at all and do not wear turbans. And if he rides a donkey out of necessity, he dismounts in crowded places and is made to pass through narrow passages.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage lays down the regulations for distinguishing dhimmis from Muslims in public life. They are to ride on pack-saddles (not on proper saddles), and they must not wear certain garments like tayalisah (a type of headscarf) and ardiya (cloaks), nor the attire of scholars and noblemen. A distinguishing mark is placed on their houses. They are not permitted to build new churches or synagogues in cities founded by Muslims (misr). There is a difference of opinion on whether they may live among Muslims; the relied-upon view allows it in a separate quarter. As for the number of distinguishing signs, the relied-upon view is that they are completely prohibited from riding (any mount) and from wearing turbans. If a dhimmi must ride a donkey out of necessity, he dismounts in crowded gatherings and is forced to pass through inconvenient routes to avoid being mistaken for a Muslim.

Key Terms
تميّز (tamyiz): Distinguishing; making something distinct from others.
مركب (markab): Riding animal or mount.
ملبس (malbas): Clothing, dress.
بيعة (bi'a): Church (place of worship for Christians).
كنيسة (kanisa): Synagogue or church (place of worship).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view, what must a dhimmi do if he rides a donkey out of necessity in a crowded place?
A) He must dismount
B) He may continue riding
C) He must wear a turban
D) He must avoid the crowd

Key Takeaway
Dhimmis must maintain outward distinction from Muslims in dress, mount, residence, and worship places to uphold the public order of a Muslim society.

beginner
6 min
Islam Wipes Out Previous Sins Except Human Rights
Rule with exceptions on conversion and sin forgiveness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on the effect of conversion to Islam on previous obligations and apply it by distinguishing between huquq Allah and huquq al-ibad, including the exceptions.

Core Idea
Islam erases obligations to Allah, not debts or retaliation to people.

When a non-Muslim converts to Islam, what happens to their past sins? The Hanafi school articulates a clear rule: sins against Allah are forgiven, but human rights remain. This lesson unpacks that principle and its two exceptions, guiding you through the distinctions that a mufti must draw.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
State that conversion to Islam wipes out obligations to Allah (huquq Allah) such as missed prayers and fasts, but does not waive obligations to other humans (huquq al-ibad) like debts, qisas, and guarantees.
2
Locate the two exceptions
Find in the passage: (1) if a kafir became junub and then converted, the janabah is not automatically removed; (2) if a kafir committed zina and the act was proven by Muslim witnesses before conversion, the hadd penalty is not dropped.
These exceptions are themselves huquq Allah that survive conversion.
3
Apply the rule to a new case
Given a scenario (e.g., a convert who stole before Islam), decide whether the obligation falls under huquq Allah or huquq al-ibad and state the ruling.
Theft violates a human right (property), so repayment is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه
الإسلام يجب ما قبله من حقوق الله تعالى دون حقوق الآدميين؛ كالقصاص وضمان الأموال إلا في مسائل : لو أجنب الكافر ثم أسلم لم تسقط. ومنها لو زنى ثم أسلم وكان زناه ثابتا ببينة مسلمين لم يسقط الحد بإسلامه وإلا سقط.
Notice: Islam wipes out what came before it of the rights of Allah, but not the rights of human beings, such as retaliation (qisas) and guarantee of property, except in certain cases: if a kafir becomes junub and then becomes Muslim, it does not fall. And among them, if he committed zina and then became Muslim, and his zina was established by the testimony of Muslim witnesses, the hadd is not dropped by his Islam; otherwise it falls.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This notice addresses the effect of conversion to Islam on previous sins. The general rule is that Islam erases all obligations owed to Allah (e.g., missed prayers, fasting, kaffarah for breaking oaths) but does not erase obligations owed to other humans, such as qisas (retaliation for murder or injury) and financial guarantees. However, there are exceptions: if a kafir was in a state of janabah (major impurity) and then converts, he does not need to perform ghusl for that janabah (it is waived). Another exception: if a kafir committed zina (unlawful sexual intercourse) and then converts, if the zina was proven by Muslim witnesses during his kufr, the hadd penalty is not dropped upon conversion; but if it was not established by such witnesses, the hadd falls.

Key Terms
حقوق الله (huquq Allah): Rights of Allah; religious obligations like prayer, fasting, etc.
حقوق الآدميين (huquq al-adamiyyin): Rights of human beings; civil obligations like debts, penalties for injury.
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation for murder or injury.
ضمان (daman): Compensation, guarantee for property.
أجنب (ajnaba): Became junub (in state of major impurity).
بينة (bayyina): Proof, evidence, especially testimony of witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a kafir becomes junub and then converts to Islam, what happens to his state of janabah?
A) It is automatically purified and he does not need ghusl
B) It remains; he must perform ghusl before praying
C) It becomes considered as a minor impurity
D) It is waived only if he repents

Key Takeaway
Conversion to Islam erases obligations to Allah but not to people; debts, qisas, and financial guarantees survive, with exceptions for janabah and hadd proven by Muslim witnesses.

Shared Rulings Among Dhimmis: Jews, Christians, and Magians
Rule on shared rulings among dhimmis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which four rulings Jews and Christians share, and which two rulings Magians share according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Jews and Christians share in four rulings; Magians share only in jizya and diya.

This passage from 'al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir' presents a concise rule for the mufti: when dealing with dhimmis, not all non-Muslims are alike. While Jews and Christians share many rulings, Magians differ in marriage and slaughter. This rule helps the mufti issue consistent fatwas across these communities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four shared rulings for Jews and Christians
The text states that Jews and Christians share in jizya, permissibility of marriage (with Muslim men), permissibility of their slaughter (dhabaih), and diya (blood money).
All four are explicitly mentioned as مشتركة between them.
2
Distinguish Magians
According to al-Bazzaziyya, Magians share with Jews and Christians only in jizya and diya, but not in marriage or slaughter.
Thus Magian women are not permissible for marriage, nor is their meat halal.
3
Note equality of dhimmis in the mentioned rulings
All dhimmis (Jews, Christians, Magians) are equal in the matters that are common: jizya and diya are the same among them.
This simplifies the mufti's ruling regarding these two issues.
4
Understand the qisas and diya implications
A Muslim is killed for killing a dhimmi (qisas applies), and the diya of a dhimmi equals that of a Muslim. However, a Muslim (or dhimmi) is not killed for killing a musta'min (temporary safe-conduct holder).
This establishes reciprocity in blood money but exempts musta'min from qisas protection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبیه آخر : اشتراك اليهود والنصارى في وضع الجزية وحلّ المنكاحة والذبائح وفي الدية. وفي البزازية : شاركهم المجوسي في الجزية والدّية دون الآخرين واستوى أهل الذمة فيما ذكر. وقتل المسلم بالذمي ودية الكافر والمسلم سواء، ولا يقتل المسلم والذمي بمستأمن .
Another notice: The Jews and Christians share in the imposition of jizya, permissibility of marriage (nikah), slaughter (dhabaih), and in diya. And in al-Bazzaziyya: The Magian shared with them in jizya and diya but not the others. And the people of dhimma are equal in what was mentioned. And a Muslim is killed for a dhimmi, and the diya of a kafir and a Muslim are equal. And a Muslim and a dhimmi are not killed for a musta'min.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This notice highlights the legal commonalities and differences among dhimmis. Jews and Christians share all four matters: jizya, marriage with Muslim men (i.e., permissible for Muslim men to marry their women), slaughter (their slaughtered animals are permissible for Muslims), and diya (blood money) is equal among them. According to al-Bazzaziyya, Magians (Zoroastrians) share with Jews and Christians in jizya and diya, but not in marriage and slaughter (i.e., Muslim men cannot marry Magian women, and their slaughtered animals are not permissible). All dhimmis are equal in the matters mentioned. Additionally, a Muslim is executed for killing a dhimmi (i.e., qisas applies), and the diya for a kafir and a Muslim are the same (according to some narrations, the diya of a dhimmi is equal to that of a Muslim). Lastly, neither a Muslim nor a dhimmi is executed for killing a musta'min (a non-Muslim temporary resident with safe conduct).

Key Terms
جزية (jizya): Poll tax imposed on dhimmis for protection.
منكحة (minkaha): Marriage (permissibility of marriage with Muslim men).
ذبائح (dhabaih): Slaughtered animals.
دية (diya): Blood money paid for manslaughter or injury.
مستأمن (musta'min): Non-Muslim given temporary safe conduct (aman) in the Islamic state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which group shares with Jews and Christians only in jizya and diya, but not in marriage or slaughter?
A) Magians
B) Pagans
C) Atheists
D) Musta'min

Key Takeaway
Jews and Christians share in jizya, marriage, slaughter, and diya; Magians share only in jizya and diya; qisas applies between Muslim and dhimmi but not for musta'min.

No Inheritance between Muslim and Kafir: The Hanafi Rule and Its Exceptions
A ruling (qa'ida) on inheritance between Muslims and non-Muslims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that there is no mutual inheritance between Muslims and non-Muslims, and apply the exceptions for inheritance among non-Muslims of different faiths and the special case of the apostate.

Core Idea
Muslims and non-Muslims never inherit from each other, but non-Muslims inherit from each other as if all disbelief is one religion, under the same abode.

This short notice from Ibn Nujaym's Al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair encapsulates a key rule in Hanafi inheritance law: the complete separation of inheritance between Muslims and non-Muslims. It also explains how inheritance operates among non-Muslims themselves, and gives a special rule for the apostate. Understanding this rule is essential for a mufti issuing fatwas on inheritance, especially in multi-religious societies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general prohibition
The text explicitly states 'no inheritance between Muslim and kafir'. This is the foundational rule: a Muslim does not inherit from a non-Muslim, nor a non-Muslim from a Muslim.
This rule is based on the hadith: 'The Muslim does not inherit from the kafir, nor the kafir from the Muslim.' (Bukhari, Muslim)
2
Understand the exception for non-Muslims
Inheritance runs among Jews, Christians, and Magians because, according to the Hanafi school, all kufr (disbelief) is considered one religion (millah wahidah) for the purpose of inheritance.
This means a Jew can inherit from a Christian, and vice versa, as long as the condition of same abode is met.
3
Note the condition: unity of abode (ittihad al-dar)
For inheritance among non-Muslims to occur, they must reside in the same Islamic state (dar). If they are in different abodes (e.g., one in a Muslim state and one in a non-Muslim state), the rule does not apply.
The condition of ittihad al-dar ensures that the legal fiction of 'one religion' does not extend across hostile territories.
4
Recognize the independent rule for apostates (murtadd)
The apostate's earnings made during his apostasy from Islam are inherited by his Muslim heirs, regardless of unity of abode. This is an exception to the general rule that a kafir's property does not go to Muslims.
This rule is specific to the apostate and does not apply to other non-Muslims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبیه آخر : لا توارث بين المسلم والكافر، ويجري الإرث بين اليهود والنصارى والمجوس، والكفر كله عندنا ملة واحدة بشرط اتحاد الدار والكفار يتعاقدون فيما بينهم، وإن اختلفت مللهم وخرج المرتد ; فإنه يرث كسب إسلامه ورثته المسلمون مع عدم الاتحاد .
Another notice: There is no inheritance between a Muslim and a kafir. And inheritance runs between Jews, Christians, and Magians. And all kufr (unbelief), according to us, is one religion, provided they share the same abode (ittihad al-dar). And the kuffar contract engagements among themselves, even if their religions differ. And as for the apostate (murtadd): his earnings from his Islam are inherited by his Muslim heirs, even without unity of abode.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This notice covers inheritance (tawaruth) rules. The general rule is that a Muslim does not inherit from a non-Muslim, nor vice versa. However, non-Muslims (Jews, Christians, Magians) can inherit from each other because, according to the Hanafi school, all disbelief is considered one religion (millah wahidah) for inheritance purposes, provided they reside in the same abode (dar), i.e., the same Islamic state. They can contract marriage and business among themselves even if their specific religions differ. There is a special case for an apostate (murtadd): if he earns wealth during his apostasy, upon his death, his Muslim heirs inherit that wealth, even if they are not in the same abode (e.g., if he apostatized in a non-Muslim land but his Muslim relatives are in a Muslim land, they still inherit).

Key Terms
توارث (tawaruth): Mutual inheritance (each inheriting from the other).
ملة واحدة (millah wahidah): One religion; legal fiction for inheritance among non-Muslims.
اتحاد الدار (ittihad al-dar): Unity of abode; living in the same Islamic state.
مرتد (murtadd): Apostate; a Muslim who leaves Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this text, why can a Jew inherit from a Christian?
A) Because they are both Muslims
B) Because all kufr is considered one religion for inheritance
C) Because they signed a contract
D) Because they live in the same house

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that no inheritance occurs between a Muslim and a non-Muslim, but non-Muslims inherit from each other as if one millah, provided they share the same abode; the apostate's earnings from his Islam go to his Muslim heirs regardless of abode.
